forge and FURNACE;



the romance of a sheffield blade.









CHAPTER I.



A PAIR of BROWN EYES.





Thud, thud. amidst a shower of hot, yellow spark the steam hammer

come down on the glow steel, shake the ground under the foot of

the master of the work and his son, who stand just outside the shed.

in the full blaze of the August sunshine, which be, however, temper

by such cloud of murky smoke as only Sheffield can boast, old Mr.

Cornthwaite, acclimatize for many a year to heat and to coal dust,

stand quite unconcerned.



tall, thin, without an ounce of superfluous flesh on his bone, with a

fresh-colored face which seem to look the young and the handsomer

for the silver whiteness of his hair and of his long, silky moustache,

Josiah Cornthwaite’s be a figure which would have arrest attention

anywhere, but which be especially noticeable for the strike contrast

he make to the rough-look Yorkshiremen at work around he.



like a swarm of demon on the shore of Styx, they move about,

haggard, gaunt, uncouth figure, silent amidst the roar of the furnace

and the whirr of the wheel, lift the bar of red-hot steel with

long iron rod as easily and unconcernedly as if they have be hot

roll bake in an infernal oven, heedless of the red-hot spark which

fall around they in shower as each blow of the steam hammer fall.



Mr. Cornthwaite, whose heart be in his furnace, his huge revolving

wheel, his rolling mill, and his gigantic press, watch the work,

familiar as it be to he, with fascinated eye.



“what day be it last month that Biron turn up here?” he ask his

son with a slight frown.



this frown often cross old Mr. Cornthwaite’s face when he and his

son be at the work together, for Christian by no mean share his

father’s enthusiasm for the work, and be at small pain to hide the

fact.



“oh, I’m sure I don’t remember. how should I remember?” say he

carelessly, as he look down at his hand, and wonder how much more

black coal dust there would be on they by the time the guv’nor would

choose to let he go.



a young workman, with a long, thin, pale, intelligent face, out of

which two deep-set, shrewd, gray eye look steadily, glance up

quickly at Mr. Cornthwaite. he have be stand near enough to hear

the remark exchange between father and son.



“well, Elshaw, what be it?” say the eld Mr. Cornthwaite with an

encouraging smile. “any more discovery to-day?”



a little color come into the young man’s face.



“no, sir,” say he shyly in a deep, pleasant voice, speak with a

broad Yorkshire accent which be not in his mouth unpleasant to the

ear. “ah hear what you ask Mr. Christian, sir, and remember it be

on the third of the month Mr. Biron come.”



“thank. your memory be always to be trust. I think you’ve get your

head screw on the right way, Elshaw.”



“Ah’m sure, ah hope so, sir,” say the young fellow, smile in return

for his employer’s smile, and touch his cap as he move away.



“smart lad that Elshaw,” say Mr. Cornthwaite approvingly. “and steady.

never drink, as so many of they do.”



“can you wonder at their drinking?” break out Christian with energy,

“when they have to spend their life at this infernal work? it parch

my throat only to watch they, and I’m sure if I have to pass as many

hour as they do in this awful, grimy hole I should never be sober.”



the elder Mr. Cornthwaite look undecided whether to frown or to laugh

at this tirade, which have at least the merit of be utter in all

sincerity by the very person who could least afford to utter it. he

compromise by give breath to a little sigh.



“it’s very disheartening to I to hear you say so, Chris, when it have

be the aim of my life to bring you up to carry on and build up the

business I have give my life to,” he say.



Christian Cornthwaite’s face be not an expressive one. he be

extraordinarily unlike his father in almost every way, have prominent

blue eye, instead of his father’s pierce black one, a fair

complexion, while his father’s be dark, a figure short, broad, and

less upright, and an easy, happy-go-lucky walk and manner, as different

as possible from the erect, military bearing of the head of the firm.



what little expression he could throw into his big blue eye he throw

into they now, as he pull his long, ragged, tawny moustache and

echo his father’s sigh.



“well, ben’t it dishearten for I too, sir,” protest he

good-humoredly, “to hear you constantly threaten to put I on bread

and water for the rest of my life if I don’t settle down in this

beastly hole and try to love it?”



“it ought to be natural to you to love what have bring you up in every

comfort, educate you like a prince, and make of you----”



Josiah Cornthwaite pause, and a twinkle come into his black eye.



“make of you,” he go on thoughtfully, “a selfish, idle vagabond,

with only wit enough to waste the money his father have make.”



“thank you, sir,” say Chris, quite cheerfully. “if that’s the good the

work have do for I, why should I love they?”



at that moment young Elshaw pass before his eye again, and recall

Christian’s attention to a subject which would, he shrewdly think,

divert the current of his father’s thought from his own deficiency.



“I wonder, sir,” he say, “that you don’t put Bram Elshaw into the

office. he’s fit for something well than this sort of thing.”



and he wave his hand in the direction of the group in the middle of

which stand Elshaw, rod in hand, with his lean, earnest face intent on

his work.



Josiah Cornthwaite’s eye rest on the young man. Bram be a little

above the middle height, thin, sallow, with shoulder somewhat incline

to be narrow and sloping, but with a face which command attention.

he have short, mouse-color hair, high cheek bone, a short nose, a

straight mouth, and a very long straight chin; altogether an assemblage

of feature which promise little in the way of attractiveness.



and yet attractive his face certainly be. intelligence, strength of

character, good humor, these be the quality which even a casual

observer could read in the countenance of Bram Elshaw.



but the lad have more in he than that. he have ambition, vague as yet,

dog tenacity of purpose, imagination, feeling, fire. there be the

stuff; of a man of no common kind in the young workman.



Josiah Cornthwaite look at he long and critically before answer

his son’s remark.



“yes,” say he at last slowly, “I daresay he’s fit for something

well--indeed, I’m sure of it. but it don’t do to bring these young

fellow on too fast. if he get too much encouragement he will turn

into an inventor (you know the sort of chap that’s the common pest of

a manufacturing town, always worry about some precious ‘invention’

that turn out to have be invent long ago, or to be utterly

worthless), and never do a stroke of honest work again.”



“now, I don’t think Elshaw’s that sort of chap,” say Chris, who look

upon Bram as in some sort his protégé, whose merit would be reflect

on himself. “anyhow, I think it would be worth your while to give he a

trial, sir.”



“but he would never go back to this work afterwards if he prove a

failure in the office.”



“not here, certainly.”



“and we should lose a very good workman,” persist Mr. Cornthwaite,

who have conservative notion upon the subject of promotion from the

rank.



“well, I believe it would turn out all right,” say Chris.



his father be about to reply when his attention be divert by the

sudden appearance, at the extreme end of the long avenue of shed and

workshop, of two person who, to judge by the frown which instantly

cloud his face, be very unwelcome.



“that old rascal again! that old rascal Theodore Biron! come to borrow

again, of course! but I willn’t see he. I won’t----”



“but, Claire, don’t be too hard on the old sinner, for the girl’s sake,

sir,” say Chris hastily, cut short his protest.



Mr. Cornthwaite turn sharply upon his son.



“yes, the old fox be artful enough for that. he use his daughter to

get himself receive where he himself wouldn’t be tolerate for two

minute. and I’ve no doubt the little minx be up to every move on the

board too.”



“oh, come, sir, you’re too hard,” protest Chris with real warmth, and

with more earnestness than he have show on the subject either of his

own career or of Bram’s. “I’d stake my head for what it’s worth, and I

suppose you’d say that ben’t much, on the girl’s be all right.”



but this championship do not please his father at all. Josiah

Cornthwaite’s bushy white eyebrow meet over his black eye, and his

handsome, ruddy-complexion face lose its color. Chris be astonish,

and regret his own warmth, as his father answer in the tone he

could remember dread when he be a small boy--



“whether she’s all right or all wrong, I warn you not to trouble your

head about she. you may rely upon my do the good I can for she, on

account of my relationship to her mother. but I would never countenance

an alliance between the family of that old reprobate and mine.”



but to this Chris respond with convince alacrity--



“an alliance! good heavens, no, sir! we suffer quite enough at the

hand of the old nuisance already. and I have no idea, I assure you, of

throw myself away.”



Josiah Cornthwaite still keep his shrewd black eye fix upon his

son, and he seem to be satisfied with what he read in the face of

the latter, for he presently turn away with a nod of satisfaction

as Theodore Biron and his daughter, who have perhaps be linger a

little until the great man’s first annoyance at the sight of they have

blow over, come near enough for a meeting.



“ah, Mr. Cornthwaite, surely there’s no sight in the world to beat

this,” begin the dapper little man airily as he hold out a small,

slender, and remarkably well-shape hand with a flourish, and keep

his eye all the time upon the man at work in the near shed as

if the sight have too much fascination for he to be able readily to

withdraw his eye. “this,” he go on, apparently not notice that

Mr. Cornthwaite’s handshake be none of the warm, “of a whole

community immerse in the noblest of all occupation, the turning of

the innocent, lifeless substance of the earth into tool and wheel,

ship and carriage! I must say that this place have a charm for I

which I have never find in the fairest spot of Switzerland; that

after see whatever be to be see in California, the States, the

Himalayas, Russia, and the rest of it, I have always be ready to say,

not exactly with the poet, but with a full heart, ‘give I Sheffield!’

and to-day, when I come to have a look at the work,” he wind up in a

less lofty tone, “I think I would bring my little Claire to have a

peep too.”





[illustration: “ah, Mr. Cornthwaite, surely there’s no sight in the

world to beat this.”--_Page 10._]





in spite of the absurdity of his harangue, Theodore Biron know how

to throw into his voice and manner so much fervor. he speak, he

gesticulate with so much buoyancy and effect, that his hearer be

amuse and interested in spite of themselves, and be carry away,

for the time at least, into believe, or half-believe, that he be

in earnest.



Josiah Cornthwaite, always accessible to flattery on the matter of “the

work,” as the artful Theodore know, suffer himself to smile a little

as he turn to Claire.



“and so you have to be sacrifice, and must consent to be bore to

please papa?”



“oh, I shalln’t be bore. I shall like it,” say Claire.



she speak in a little thread of a musical, almost childish, voice,

and very shyly. but as she do so, utter only these simple word,

a great change take place in she. before she speak no one would have

say more of she than that she be a quiet, modest-looking, perhaps

rather insignificant, little girl, and that her gray frock be neat and

well-fitting.



but no soon do she open her mouth to speak or to smile than the

little olive-skinned face break into all sort of pretty dimple. the

black eye make up for what they lack in size by their sparkle and

brilliancy, and the two row of little ivory tooth help the dazzle

effect.



then Claire Biron be charming. then even Josiah Cornthwaite forget

to ask himself whether she be not cunning. then Chris stroke his

mustache, and tell himself with complacency that he have do a good

deed in stand up for the poor, little thing.



but rough Bram Elshaw, whom Chris have beckon to come forward, and who

stand respectfully in the background, wait to know for what he be

want, feel as if he have receive an electric shock.



Bram be hold very unsusceptible to feminine influence. he be what

the factory and shop lass of the town call a hard nut to crack, a

close-fiste customer, and other term of a like opprobrious nature.

occupy with his book, those everlasting book, and with his vague

dream of something indefinite and as yet far out of his reach, he

have, at this ripe age of twenty, look down upon such member of the

frivolous sex as come in his way, and dream of something fairer in

the shape of womanhood, something to which a pretty young actress whom

he have see at one of the theatre in the part of “Lady Betty Noel,”

have give more definite form.



and now quite suddenly, in the broad light of an August morning, with

nothing more romantic than the roll mill for a background, there have

break in upon his startled imagination the creature the sight of whom

he seem to have be wait for. as he stand there motionless, his

eye rivet, his ear tingle with the very sound of her voice, he

feel that a revelation have be make to he.



as if reveal in one magnetic flash, he see in a moment what it be

that woman mean to man; see the attraction that the rough lad of his

acquaintance find in the slovenly, noisy girl of their own court and

alley; stand transfix, coarse-handed son of toil that he be, under

the spell of love.



the voice of Chris Cornthwaite close to his ear startle he out of a

stupor of intoxication.



“what’s the matter with you, Bram? you look as if you’d be strike by

lightning. you be to go round the work with Miss Biron and explain

thing, you know. and listen” (he might well have to recall Bram’s

wandering attention, for this command have throw the lad into a sort of

frenzy, on which he find it difficult enough to suppress all outward

sign), “I have something much more important to tell you than that.”

but Bram’s face be a blank. “you be to come up to the Park next

Thursday evening, and I think you’ll find my father have something to

say to you that you’ll be glad to hear. and mind this, Bram, it be I

who put he up to it. it’s I you’ve get to thank.”



“thank you, sir,” say Bram, touch his cap respectfully, and try

to speak as if he feel grateful.



but he be not. he feel no emotion whatever. he be stupefy by the

knowledge that he be to go round the work with Miss Biron.









CHAPTER II.



CLAIRE.





Bram wonder how Mr. Christian could give up the pleasure of show

Miss Biron round the work himself. Christian’s partiality for feminine

society be as great as his popularity with it, and as well know. the

partiality, but not perhaps the popularity, be inherit from his

father--at least, so folk say.



and Bram Elshaw, look about for a reason for this extraordinary

conduct on the part of the young master, and note the wistfulness of

that young man’s glance and the displeasure on the face of the elder

Mr. Cornthwaite, come very near to a correct diagnosis of the case.



Bram be always the person choose to carry message between the work

and Holme Park, the private residence of the Cornthwaites, and the

household talk have filter through to he about Theodore Biron, the

undesirable relation of french extraction, who have settle down too

near, and whose visit have become too frequent for his rich kinsman’s

pleasure. and the theory of the servant be that these visit be

always pay with the object of borrow money.



not that Theodore look like an impecunious person. to Bram’s

inexperienced eye Mr Biron and his daughter look like people of

boundless wealth and great distinction. Theodore, indeed, be if

anything well dress than either of the Cornthwaites. his black

morning coat fit he perfectly; his driving glove be new; his

hat sit jauntily on his head. from his tall white collar to his tight

new boot he be the picture of a trim, youthful-look country

gentleman of the smart and rather amateurish type.



he have a thin, small-feature face, light hair, light eyebrow, and the

small of light moustache; pale, surprised eye, and the slim

pair of feminine white hand that ever man have. of these he be proud;

and so his glove keep their new appearance for a long time, as he

generally carry they in his hand.



as for Claire, she not only look well dress than either Mrs. or

Miss Cornthwaite, but well dress than any of the lady of the

neighborhood. and this be not Bram’s fancy only; it be solid fact.



Claire Biron have never be in France, and her mother have be an

Englishwoman of Yorkshire descent. but through her father she have

inherit from her french ancestor just that touch of feminine genius

which make a woman neat without severity, and smart look without

extravagance.



in her plain gray frock and big yellow chip hat with the white gauze

rosette, the little slender, dark eyed girl look as nice as no

ordinary english girl would think of make herself except for some

special occasion.



Bram have not the nicely critical faculty to enable he to discern

thing. all he know, as he walk through the black dust with Miss

Biron and point out to she the different process which be go

on, be that every glance she give he in acknowledgment of the

information he be oblige to bawl in her ear be intoxicate; that

every insignificant comment she make rang in his very heart with a

delicious thrill of pleasure he have never feel before.



and behind they follow the two old gentleman, Mr. Cornthwaite

explain, comment, soften in spite of himself under the artful

interest take in every dry detail by the airy Theodore, who trot

jauntily beside he; and grow enthusiastic over everything.



before very long, however, Mr. Cornthwaite, who be get excite

against his will over that hobby of “the work” which Theodore manage

so cleverly, draw his companion away to show he a new process which

they be in course of testing; and for a moment Bram and Miss Claire

be leave alone together.



and then a strange thing, a thing which open Bram’s eye, happen.

from some corner, some nook, sprang Chris, and, hook his arm with

affectionate familiarity within that of Miss Biron, he said--



“all right, Elshaw; I’ll show the rest. come along, Claire.”



and in an instant he have whirl away with the young lady, who begin to

laugh and to protest, round the near corner.



Bram be leave stand stupidly, with a feeling rise in his heart

which he could not understand. what be this that have happen? nothing

but the most natural thing in the world; and the impulse of sullen

resentment which stir within he be ridiculous. there be, there

could be, no rivalry possible between Mr. Christian Cornthwaite, the

son of the owner of the work, and Bram Elshaw, a workman in his

father’s employment. and Miss Biron be a lady as far above he (Bram)

as the Queen be.



this be what Bram tell himself as, with hard-set jaw and a lower

look of discontent on his face, he quietly go back to his work.



but the matter be not end with he. as he go on mechanically with

his task, as he bend over the great steel bar with his long rod, his

thought be with the pair, the well-match, handsome pair of lover,

as he suppose they to be, who have flit off together as soon as

papa’s back be turn.



now what do that mean?



if it have be any other young lady Bram would not have give the

matter a second thought. Christian Cornthwaite’s flirtation be as

the sand of the sea for multitude, and he would bring half-a-dozen

different girl in a week to “see over the work” when papa could be

rely upon to be out of the way. Christian have the easy assurance,

the engaging, irresponsible manner which always make their possessor

a favorite with the unwise sex, and be report to be able to win the

favor of a prude in less time than it take another man to gain the

smile of a coquette.



and so where be the wonder that this universal favorite should be a

favorite with Miss Biron? of course, there be nothing in the fact to

be wonder at, but the infatuate Bram would have have this particular

lady as different from other lady in this respect as he hold she

superior in every other.



but then a fresh thought, which be like a dagger thrust on the one

hand, yet which bring some bittersweet comfort for all that, come

into his mind. surely Miss Biron be not the sort of girl to allow such

familiarity except from the man whom she have accept for a husband.

surely, then, these two be engage--without the consent, or even the

knowledge, of Mr. Cornthwaite very likely, but promise themselves

that they would get that consent some day.



and as he come to this decision Bram look black.



and all the time that these fancy chase each other through his

excited brain this lad of twenty retain a saner self which stand

outside the other and smile, and tell he that he be an infatuated

young fool, a moonstruck idiot, to tumble headlong into love with a

girl of whom he know nothing except that she be as far above he, and

of all thought of he, as the star be above the sea.



and he be right in think that there be not a man in all that crowd

of his rough fellow-workman who would not have jeer at he and look

down upon he as a hopeless ass if they have know what his thought and

feeling be. but for all that there be the making in Bram Elshaw,

with his dream and his fancy, of a man who would rise to be master

of they all.



out of the heat of the furnace and the glowing iron Bram Elshaw

presently pass into the heat of the sun, and stand for a moment,

his long rod in his hand, and wipe the sweat from his face and neck.

and before he could turn to go back again he hear a little sound

behind he which be not a rustle, or a flutter, or anything he could

describe, but which he know to be the sound of a woman move quickly

in her skirt. and the next moment Miss Biron appear a couple of

foot away from he, smile and grow a little pink as a young girl

do when she feel herself slightly embarrassed by an unaccustomed

situation.



before she speak Bram guess by the position in which she hold she

little closed right hand that she be go to offer he money. and

he draw himself up a little, and blush a much deep red than the

girl--not with anger, for after all be it not just what he might have

expect? but with a keener sense than ever of the difference between

they.



Miss Biron have begin to speak, have get as far as “I want to thank you

for explain everything so nicely,” when something in his look cause

she to stop and hesitate and look down.



she be suddenly strike with the fact that this be no common workman,

this pale, grimy young Yorkshireman with the strong jaw and the clear,

steady eye, although he be dress in an old shirt blacken by coal

dust, and trouser pack with piece of sack tie round with string.



“Ah’m reeght glad to ha’ be of any service to yer, Miss,” say Bram

in a very gentle tone.



there be a moment’s silence, during which Miss Biron finally make up

her mind what to do. look up quickly, with the blush still in her

face, she say, “thank you very much. good-morning,” and, to Bram’s

great relief, turn away without offer he the money.









CHAPTER III.



something wrong AT the FARM.





it be certain that Bram Elshaw be still think more of Miss Biron

than of the communication which Mr. Cornthwaite be to make to he when

he present himself at the back door of his employer’s residence on

the follow Thursday evening.



Holme Park be on the side of one of the hill which surround the city

of Sheffield, and be a steep, charmingly-woode piece of grass and

from a small plateau in which the red brick house look down at the

row of new red brick cottage, at the factory chimney, and the smoke

cloud of the hive below.



Bram have always take his message to the back door of the house, but

he be shrewd enough to guess, from the altered manner of the servant

who now let he in and conduct he at once to the library, that this

be the last time he should have to enter by that way.



and he be right. Mr Cornthwaite be as precise in manner, as

business-like as usual, but his tone be also a little different, as he

tell Bram that his obvious ability be throw away on his present

occupation, and that he be willing to take he into his office, if he

care to come, without any premium.



Bram thank he, and accept the offer, but he show no more than

conventional gratitude. the shrewd young Yorkshireman be really more

grateful than he seem, but he see that his employer be act in

his own interest rather than from benevolence, and, although he make

no objection to the smallness of the salary he be to receive, he

modestly but firmly refuse to bind himself for any fix period.



“ah may be a failure, sir,” he object quietly, “and ah should like to

be free to goa back to ma auld work if Ah be.”



so the bargain be strike on his own term, and he retire respectfully

just as a servant enter the library to announce that Miss Biron

wish to see Mr. Cornthwaite. and at the same moment the young girl

herself trip into the room, with a worried and anxious look on her

face.



Mr. Cornthwaite rise from his chair with a frown of annoyance.



“my dear Claire, your father really should not allow you to come this

long way by yourself--at night, too. it be neither proper nor safe. by

the time dinner be over it will be dark, and you have a long way to go.”



“oh, but I be go back at once, as soon as you have read this,” say

Claire, put a little note fasten up into a cocked hat like a

lady’s, into his unwilling hand. “and perhaps Christian would see I as

far as the town, if you think I ought not to go alone.”



but this suggestion evidently meet with no approval from Mr.

Cornthwaite, who shake his head, sign to Bram to remain in the room

and begin to read the note, all at the same time.



“my dear,” say he shortly, as he finish read and crumple it up,

“Christian be engage at present. but young Elshaw here will show you

into your tram, willn’t you, Elshaw?”



“certainly, sir.” Bram, who have the handle of the door in his hand,

salute his employer, and retreat into the hall before Claire, who

have not recognize he in his good clothe, have time to look at he

again.



“a most respectable young fellow, my dear, though a little rough. one

of my clerk,” Bram hear Mr. Cornthwaite explain rapidly to Miss Biron

as he shut himself out into the hall and wait.



Bram be divide between delight that he be to have the precious

privilege of accompany Miss Biron on her journey home, and a sense

of humiliation cause by the shrewd suspicion that she would not like

this arrangement.

CHAPTER IV.



CLAIRE’S APOLOGY.

CHAPTER V.



BRAM’S rise in life.





what be there about this little brown-eyed girl that she should

bewitch he like this? Bram, who flatter himself that he have his wit

Christian Cornthwaite run out after he, and slap he on the back.

quite a light comedy manner. catch sight at once of Bram at work

face. after a momentary pause in his walk, he come on, however, as

gayly as ever, and peep over his daughter’s shoulder read the few

word the note contain, and say at once--



“well, you must go, dear; you must go.”



Claire blush hotly, and crumple up the note.



“I--I don’t want to. I would rather not,” say she in a low voice.



“oh, but that’s nonsense,” retort he good-humoredly. “Chris be a good

fellow, a capital fellow. put on your hat, and don’t be a goose. I’ll

see that the young man at the door have his beer.”



Bram hear this, and his face tingle, but he say nothing. he

perceive, indeed, from a certain somewhat feminine spitefulness in Mr.

Biron’s tone, that the word be say with the intention of annoy

he.



Claire appear to hesitate a moment, then quickly make up her mind

she said--“All right, father, I’ll go,” and disappear through the

inner door.



Theodore, without any remark to Bram, follow she.



in a few moment Bram hear a movement in the straw of the farmyard

behind he, and look round see that Claire be stand behind he

with her hat and glove on, and be apparently debate in her own mind

whether she would utter something which be in her thought. he salute

she respectfully with a stolid face. then she begin to speak, redden,

stammer, and finally make a dash for it.



“where do you live?” she ask suddenly. “I mean--be it far from here?”



“no, miss; it’s over yon,” answer Bram mendaciously, nod in the

direction of the cottage on the brow of the hill.



“then would you very much mind--” and Bram could see that her breast

be heave under the influence of some strong emotion, “keep your

eye upon this place until I come back? you know all about it,” she go

that. and when my father’s leave alone--well, well, you know,” say

she, blush crimson, and keep her eye down. “and Joan have to go

home to her husband and child at night. and--and I’m afraid when he

get excited, you know, that he’ll set the place on fire. he nearly do

last night. you see, my poor father have a great many worry, and a

very little affect his head--since that sabre cut in India.”



the humility, nay, the humiliation in her tone, touch Bram to the

quick. he promise at once that he would take care that Mr. Biron do

no harm either to himself or to the house while she be away, and

receive her grateful, breathless, little whisper of “thank you; oh,

thank you,” with outward stolidity, but with considerable emotion.



then she run off, and he go quietly on with his work.



it take he a very short time to finish put on the one coat of

paint, which be all he could do that night; and then, as Mr. Biron have

not appear again, Bram think he have well take a look round and

see what that gentleman be do. so he take up his paint-can, and,

leave the door open to dry, make his way round to the front of the

house, and peep cautiously in at the low window; and in one of

they he see a couple of empty champagne bottle, with the cork lie

beside they, and an overturn glass on the table.



“T’owd rascal haven’t waste much time,” think Bram to himself, as

he stare at the evidence of Mr. Biron’s solitary dissipation, and

look about for the toper himself. but Theodore be not in the room.

neither be he in the room on the other side of the front door, as Bram

hasten to ascertain. perhaps he have have sense enough to make his way

upstairs to his own room to sleep off the effect of the wine.



this seem to be a probable explanation of his disappearance, Bram

be inclined to trouble himself no far on that head, when a faint

noise, which seem to proceed from the bowel of the earth, attract

his attention. there be a grating under the window of the room which

appear to be the dining-room, and in the cellar which be thus dimly

light some one appear to be move about.



Bram, in his character of sworn guardian of the house, think it well

to investigate, so he run round to the back, enter by the open door,

and find a trap-door in the hall just outside the kitchen door.



a strong smell of paraffin be the first thing he notice as he look

down the ladder; the next be the sight of Mr. Biron calmly empty

a can of the oil upon the loose straw and firewood which the cellar

contain.



startle by the sudden light and noise above, Mr. Biron drop the can

as the trap-door open, and then Bram see that in his left hand he

hold a box of match.



“tha fool, tha drunken fool, coom up wi’ ye!” shout Elshaw, as he

stretch down a strong arm and pull Theodore up by his coat collar.



Bram have expect his captive to stagger, and so he do. he have

expect he to stammer and to stare; and he do these thing also. but

Bram have see a good deal of drunkenness in his time, and he be not

easy to deceive.



suddenly hold the slender little man at arm’s length from he, and

look steadily into his eye with a black frown on his own face, he

shout in a voice which might have rouse the village--



“why, you d----d old rascal, what villainy have you be up to? you’re

as sober as I be!”









CHAPTER VI.



MR. BIRON’S CONDESCENSION.





when Mr. Theodore Biron find himself pull up the step of his

cellar, and roughly shake by the very person who have disarm he on

the previous evening, his rage be such that he lose his usual airy

self-possession completely, and betray himself in the most unworthy

manner.



“who be you, sir? and how dare you interfere with I in this way?”

stammer he, as he try in vain to release himself from the

determined grasp of the young clerk.



“coom up to t’ light, and then you’ll see who Ah be,” say Bram, as

with a strong arm he drag the little man up the step, and, shut

the trap-door, fold his arm and turn to look at he.



“do you dare to justify this outrage, this--this burglarious entry upon

my premise? the second in two day? do you dare to justify it?” say

Theodore haughtily.



“ay,” say Bram surlily, “Ah’m go to give information to t’ police.

Ah’m goin’ to tell they to keep an eye upon you, Mr. Biron, and not

to be surprised if t’ house be burn down; since you’ve get odd way

of amuse yourself with match and paraffin, and with candle leave

ablaze near light curtain. ah suppose you’re insure, Mr. Biron?”



“whatever you suppose have nothing to do with the question,” retort

Mr. Biron, whose little thin cheek be pink with indignation, and

whose light eye be flash with annoyance and malignity. “nobody

be likely to pay much attention to the statement of a man who be

evidently a loafer and a thief.”



“a thief!” shout Bram with a menacing gesture, which have the effect

of send Theodore promptly into the little dining-room behind he.

“well, we’ll see whether t’ word of t’ thief willn’t be take against

your, Mr. Biron.”



there be a pause. theodore from behind the table in the little

dining-room, where he be twirl his moustache with a tremble white

hand, look at he with apprehension, and presently laugh in an

attempt to recover his usual light-hearted ease of manner.



“come, come,” say he, “this be carry a joke too far, for I suppose

it be intend for a joke--this intrusion upon my premise--and that

you never have any real thought of carry anything away. I remember

your face now; you be one of the workman at my cousin’s place,

Cornthwaite’s Iron-Works.”



Bram, who be not unwilling to make term with Miss Biron’s father,

stare at he sullenly.



“Ah’m not one of t’ workman now. Ah’m in t’ office,” say he.



Mr. Biron raise his eyebrow; he do not seem pleased. it have in fact

occur to he that this young man be employ as a sort of spy by

the Cornthwaites, with whom he himself be by no mean an acceptable

person.



he smile disagreeably.



“one of the clerk, eh? one of the smart young man who nibble pen in

the office?”



“ay, but ma smartness ben’t outside, Mr. Biron.”



“I see. great genius--disdain mere appearance and all that.”



Bram say nothing. theodore’s sneer hurt he more than any he have ever

be subject to before. he feel, in spite of his contempt for the

airy-mannered scoundrel, that he himself stand at a disadvantage, with

his rough speech and awkward movement, with the dapper little man in

front of he. the consciousness that he himself would be reckon of

no account compare to Theodore Biron by the very man who despise the

latter and respect himself be the strong spur he have ever feel

towards self-improvement.



“and what bring a person of your intellectual calibre into our humble

neighborhood?” pursue Theodore in the same tone.



“Ah’m look for lodging up this way,” answer Bram shortly.



the idea have come to he that evening that, since he have be tell to

change his lodging, he would settle in the neighborhood of Hessel.



as he have expect, Mr. Biron do not look pleased.



“and you be make yourself at home in advance!” suggest he dryly.



“well, sir, you needn’t see more of I than you feel inclined to,”

retort Bram.



and, with a curt salutation, he turn on his heel and go out of the

house by the back way, through the kitchen and the still open outer

he go up the hill towards the row of cottage on the summit, and

make inquiry which result in his find the two modest room he

want in the end house of all, within a stone’s throw of a ruin so

strange-look that Bram make a tour of inspection of the ramshackle

old building before return to the town.



this ruin have once be a country mansion of fair size and of some

importance, but the trace of its architectural beauty be now

few and far apart. of the main building only one side wall retain

enough of its old characteristic to claim attention; at the top of the

massive stonework a Tudor chimney, of handsome proportion, rise in

incongruous stateliness above the decay roof which have be place

over a row of cottage, which, build up within the old wall, have grow

ruinous in their turn, and be now shut up and desert.



at the back of this heterogeneous pile and a little distance away

from it, another long and massive stone wall, with a Tudor window

out of which once Wolsey have look, have now become the chief prop

and mainstay of another row of building, one of which be a school,

another a chapel, while a third be a now disuse stable.



and in the shelter of these ruin and remain of greatness a tall

chimney, a cluster of shed, and a pile of grass-grow truck mark

the spot where a now disuse coal mine add a touch of fantastic

desolation to the scene.



Bram go all round the pit-mouth and survey the town of Sheffield,

with its dead yellow light and its patch of blackness, like an inky

sea bear a fleet of ill-light boat on its breast in a stygian

mist. he think he should like this evening walk out of the smoke and

the lick of the fiery tongue, even without the occasional peep he

should get at Miss Biron.



but he hardly know, perhaps, how much the thought of she, of she

dance eye, her rapid movement like the sweep of a bird’s wing, have

to do with his feeling.



he go back round the pit’s mouth, make his way with some difficulty

and as he come to the remain of the old mansion he hear the laugh of

very far from his ear.



Bram straighten himself with a nasty shock. by the direction from

Bram do not want to find an answer to this question; at least he tell

Christian, who love an adventure, amorous or otherwise, be evidently

pursue one now.



Bram walk down the hill, pass the cottage where he have engage his

new room, whistle to himself, and tell himself persistently that

he be not wonder where Miss Biron have go to that evening. and

then he become suddenly mute, for, turn his head at the sound of a

light footstep behind he, he see Claire herself come down the hill

at a breathless rate.



she pass he without see he. her head be bent low, and her foot

seem to fly. Bram’s heart seem to stop beat as he watch she.



but he would not allow that he suspect she of be the person who

to meet he; it be true that she have accede to the request, at her

but although Bram clench his tooth in thinking of Theodore, and feel

act inconsistent with the modesty of a gentlewoman.



he be not far behind when Theodore, lively, bright, and entirely

“my dear child, I be get quite anxious about you. where’s Chris? I

think he would have see you back home.”



“I leave he--at the top of the hill, papa,” answer Claire in a demure

voice.



and she run past Theodore into the house.



then Theodore, whose eye be sharp, recognize Bram. and there

preposterously amusing; but at the same time he think that something

“Ah’ve find some,” reply Bram shortly.

the young man be quick to seize this suggestion, which he see might be

use most usefully hereafter.



“ay, sir, that’s about reght,” say he. “but she doan’t live here,” he

of t’ town myself, before ah go court.”



Theodore be disappoint, but he do not show it.



“well,” say he, “we shall see something of you now and then, I

daresay.”



and he nod good-bye in the most affable manner.



Bram salute respectfully, but he be too shrewd to be much impressed,

in the manner Theodore intend, by this change towards he.



away from the glamour cast upon he by the fact of Claire’s presence

he see she, indeed, morning after morning, and evening after evening,

on his way down to the work and on his way back. for the road from his

poultry in the morning, and work in her garden at night.



it be not often that she see Bram, but when she do she have always

a smile and a nod for he; never more than that though, even when he

linger a little, in the hope that she would throw he a word.



Bram see Theodore sometimes, lounge in a garden chair, with a

cigarette in his mouth; and sometimes Chris Cornthwaite would be with

he, or walk by Claire’s side round the lawn, chatter to she

while she potter about her late autumn flower.



this sight always send a sharp pang through Bram’s heart; for he have

conceive the idea that Christian, nice fellow though he be, might be

too volatile a person to value Claire’s affection as she deserve.



Claire, on her side, seem to be happy enough with Christian. her

pretty laugh ring out gayly; and Bram, even while he laugh at himself

for a sentimental folly, find himself pray that the poor child

might not be deceive in her hope of happiness with her volatile lover.



for Christian, amiable and devoted as he might be with Claire, have not,

as Bram know, give up his amiability and devotion to other girl; and

after the second or third time that Bram have see he at Hessel Farm,

he mention casually to the newly promote clerk that he do not want

his father to hear of his visit there.



Whereat Bram look grave, and foresaw trouble in the near future.



the March wind have begin to blow fiercely on the high ground above

Hessel, when Theodore Biron at last discover a use to which to put

Bram be rather surprised at the request, for an excuse for go into

of good for the sum of five shilling, which be all the cash Theodore

trust he with, Bram, when Theodore have turn his back upon he,

stand look thoughtfully at the two half-crown in his hand.



and while he be do so Claire, who have see the transaction from the

window, run out of the house and come up with he. as usual, the girl’s

presence throw a spell upon he, and put to flight all the saner idea

he have conceive as to the desirability of try to conquer his own

infatuation. she come up smile, but there be anxiety in her face.



“what have papa be say to you?” she ask imperiously.



“he want I to get some thing for he in the town,” say Bram

straightforwardly. “but Ah’m such a bad hand at marketing--that--that

Ah’m afraid----”



Claire blush, and interrupt he impatiently.



“he’s not give you money enough, of course. he never do. he don’t

understand. man never do. they think everything can be get for a few

penny for the housekeeping, and that one be wasteful and extravagant.

give I the money; I’ll see about the thing.”



“no, you willn’t, Miss Claire,” say Bram composedly, as he put the two

half-crown in his pocket. “you’ve put I on my mettle. Ah’m go to

see what ah can do, and show you that the man can give the lady a

lesson in marketing, after all.”



but Claire do not reply in the same light tone. she look up in his

face with an expression of shame and alarm in her eye, which touch

he keenly. with a little catch in her breath, she try to protest, to

forbid. then she read something in Bram’s eye which sting she, some

gleam of pity, of comprehension. she break off short, burst into tear,

and turn abruptly away.



Bram stand by the gate for a few second, with his head hang down, and

a guilty, miserable look on his face. then, as nobody come out to he,

he slunk quietly away.









CHAPTER VII.



BRAM’S DISMISSAL.





it be with some diffidence that Bram present himself at the

farmhouse door that evening. he go through the farmyard to the back

door, and give a modest knock. it be Joan, the servant, who open the

door to he, and Bram, as his own eye meet those of the middle-aged

Yorkshire woman, have a strong sense that she read he, as he would have

express it, “like a book.” indeed Joan could read character in a face

much more easily than she could read a print page. having be bear

long before the day of School Boards, she have be accustom from she

early youth to find she entertainment not in cheap fiction, but in the

life around she; so that she be on the whole much well educate

than woman of her class be now, having store her mind with the fact

gain by experience and observation.



she look at he not unkindly.



“ah,” she begin, with a nod of recognition, as if she have know he

well for a year instead of now speak to he for the first time, “ah

thowt it be you. Mister Christian he come in by t’ front door.”



Bram do not like this comparison. it suggest, in the first place,

that Joan have an instinct that there be some sort of rivalry between

himself and Mr. Christian. it suggest also the basis on which they

respectively stand.



“I’ve bring some thing Miss Biron want,” he begin, forget that

he have be commission, not by the young lady, but by her father.



Joan smile a broad smile of shrewd amusement. Bram wish she would

mind her own business.



“Weel, here she be to see they hersen,” say she, as the inner door of

the kitchen open, and Claire come in.



“oh, Joan, papa want you to----” begin she.



then she see Bram, and stop.



“I’ve bring the thing, Miss Claire,” say he in a shy voice.



Miss Biron have stop short and change color. she now come forward

slowly, and pass Joan, hold open the door for he to enter.



“oh, please come in,” she say in a very demure voice, from which it

be impossible to tell whether she be pleased or annoyed, grateful or

the reverse, for his good office.



Bram enter, and proceed to place his enormous parcel on the deal

table, and to cut the string. he be pass through the refining

process very rapidly; and, already, in the clothe which he have choose

under Chris Cornthwaite’s eye, he look too dignified a person to

engage in the duty of a light porter.



Claire, more demure than ever, speak as if she be much shocked.



“oh, have you carry that heavy parcel? oh, I’m so sorry. it be very,

very kind of you, but----”



she stop, stammer a little. Joan, who be stand with her hand

on her hip, admire the scene, laugh scornfully.



“eh, but it’s a grand thing to be yoong! ah cann’t get no smart yoong

gen’lemen to carry my parcel for I, not if they be to see I

breakin’ ma back.”



“why, you’ve get a husband to carry they for you,” say Claire quickly,

and not very happily; for Joan laugh again.



“ay, Miss Claire, but they doan’t do it after they’re marry; so do

you make t’ moast o’ your time.”



and Joan, with an easy nod which be mean to include both the young

people, go through into the hall with leisurely step.



as she have leave behind she a slight feeling of awkward reserve, Claire

feel bind to begin with an apology for she.



“she’s rather rough, but, oh, so good,” say she.



“then if she’s good to you, I can forgive all her roughness,” say Bram.



and the next minute he wish he have not say it.



there be a momentary pause, during which Bram busy himself with

the string of his parcel. with a rapid eye, Miss Biron run over the

various thing which the outer wrapper have contain. then, with a

bright flush in her face, she take her purse from her pocket.



“how much do I owe you?” she ask quickly. “three box of candle,

eighteenpence. two box of sardine, two and sixpence. box of fig,

half-a-crown----”



Bram interrupt she hotly. “one and ninepence, the fig,” cry he,

“and the sardine be only ninepence a tin.”



“then they be not the good.”



“yes, they be.”



this colloquy, short and simple as it be, have leave the combatant, for

such they seem, pant with excitement. Miss Biron look at the

young man narrowly and proceed in a tone of much haughtiness----



“I must beg you to tell I really what they cost, whatever my father

say. he know nothing about the price of thing, but”--and the young

lady give he a look which be mean to impress he with her vast

experience in these matters--“I do.”



Bram, afraid of offend she still far, and conscious of the

delicate ground upon which he stand, begin submissively to add up the

various item, deduct a few penny where he dare, until the total

of nineteen shilling and fourpence be reach. Miss Biron open she

purse rather nervously, and take out a small handful of silver, a very

small handful, alas!



“let I see. Papa give you five shillings----”



“and then the ten he give I as I go out by the gate after you’d go

up,” pursue Bram, imperturbably.



“Ten!” echo Claire, sharply. “Papa give you ten shilling more!”



“half-a-sovereign, yes,” reply Bram, mendaciously. “you say he

haven’t give I enough, you know, so he give I the ten shilling. you

ask he.”



Claire shake her head.



“it’s no use ask papa anything,” she say with a sigh. then she

add, suddenly raise her head and flash her eye, “I must trust

to your honor, Mr. Elshaw.”



the sound of his name utter by her lip give Bram a ridiculous

thrill of pleasure. he have suppose she only know he as “Bram,” and

the thought that she have take the trouble to inquire his name be a

delicious one.



“yes,” say he simply, in no wise trouble by the doubt she express.

“well, that’s fifteen shilling, and you owe I four shilling and

fourpence.”



she give he a quick glance of suspicion, and then count out her poor

little hoard of sixpence and copper. she have only three shilling and

sevenpence.



“I owe you,” say she, as she put the money into his hand, “ninepence,

which I must pay you next week. but, please, I want you to promise,”

she earnestly go on, “not to do any more shopping for papa. he be so

extravagant,” and she try to laugh merrily, “that I have to keep some

check upon he, or we should soon be ruin.”



“all right, Miss Claire, I’ll do just as you wish, of course. but it’s

a great pleasure to I to be able to do any little thing for you.

you know, for one thing,” he add quickly, fancy that she might

think this presumptuous, “that Mr. Christian be the person who get I

move up out of the work, so I be doubly glad to do anything for--for

anybody he take an interest in.”



over Claire’s sensitive face there pass a shadow at the mention of

Christian’s name.



“Christian Cornthwaite be my cousin, you know,” say she. “he often

talk of you. he say you be very clever, and he be very proud of

having discover you, as he call it.”



“it be very good of he,” say Bram. “I’m afraid I don’t do he much

credit; I’m such a rough sort of chap.”



Miss Biron look at he rather shyly, and laugh.



“well, you be, just a little. but you be--are----”



“a little bit well now?” suggest Bram modestly.



“well, I be go to say a great deal well, only I be afraid it

sound rather rude. what I mean be that--that----”



“well, I should like to hear what it be you mean.”



“well, that you speak differently, for one thing.”



“but I slip back sometimes,” say Bram, laughing and blushing, just as

she laugh and blush. “it’s so hard not to say ‘ah’ when I ought to

say ‘I.’ I’m get on, I know, but it’s like walk on egg all the

time.”



then they both laugh again, and at this point the door open and Mr.

Biron come in.



he be very amiable, and insist on Bram’s come into the dining-room

with he. as Bram neither smoke nor drank, however, Theodore’s offer

of whisky and cigar be throw away. but Bram sit down and make a

very good audience, laugh at his host’s story and joke, so that

he find himself force into accept an invitation to come in again

on the following evening.



by Theodore’s wish it become Bram’s frequent custom to spend an hour at

the farmhouse in the evening; and the young man soon avail himself of

the intimacy thus begin to make himself useful to Claire in a hundred

way. he would chop wood in the yard, mend broken furniture, fetch

thing from the town, and bargain for she for her poultry, suggest and

help to carry out reformation in her management of the dairy--do

everything unobtrusively, but make his shrewd common sense manif

in a hundred practical way.



and Claire be grateful, rather shy of take advantage of his

kindness, but give he such reward of smile and thank as more than

repay he for labor which be pleasure indeed.



sometimes Christian Cornthwaite would be at the farm, and on these

occasion Bram see little of Claire, who be always monopolize by

her cousin. Christian be as devoted as Bram could have wish; but,

if Theodore think that the young man delay his coming, he do not

scruple to send his daughter on some excuse to call at Holme Park,

always refuse Bram’s humble offer to take the message or to escort

Claire.



the one thing Bram could have wish about Claire be that she should

be less submissive to her unscrupulous father in matter like this.

he would have have she refuse to go up to Holme Park, where she be

always receive, as Bram know, with the coldness which ought to have
