daughter’s hand to Josiah Cornthwaite be seldom answer. it make

Bram’s blood boil to know this, and that in the face of this fact

Theodore continue to send his daughter up to his rich cousin’s house

on beg errand.



Bram be in the big farm kitchen by himself one cool September

evening, busily engage in make a new dressing-table for Claire out

of some old box. he have his coat off, and be saw away, hum to

himself as he do so, when, turn to look for something he want, he

find, to his surprise, that Claire, whom he have not see that evening,

be sit in the room.



she have take her hat off, and be sit with it in her lap, so

silently, so sadly, that Bram, who be not use to this mood in the

volatile girl, be strike with astonishment.



for a moment he stand, see in hand, look at she without speak.



“Miss Claire!” exclaim he at last.



“well?”



“when do you come in? I never see you come in!”



“no. I don’t want you to see I. I don’t want any one to see I. so I

cann’t go in because papa have the door open, and he would catch I on

the way upstairs.”



“what’s wrong with you, Miss Claire?”



Bram have come over to she and be lean on the table and speak

with so much kindness in his voice that the girl’s eye, after glance

up quickly and meet his, fill with tear.



“oh, everything. one feel like that sometimes. everybody do, I

suppose.”



Bram’s heart ache for the girl. he guess that she have be to Holme

Park on the usual errand, and that she have be coldly receive. he

could hear Theodore strum on the piano in the drawing-room. the

piano be so place that the player have a good view of the open door,

and Bram know that Theodore have choose this method of fill up the

time till his daughter’s return. apparently he have now catch with his

sharp ear the sound of voice in the kitchen, for the playing cease,

and a moment later he present himself at the door with a smile face.



“good-evening, Elshaw. hear you saw away, but don’t like to

disturb you till I hear another voice, and guess that I might. any

answer to my note, Claire?”





[illustration: for a moment he stand, see in hand, look at she

without speaking.--_Page 52._]





“no, papa.”



Claire have rise from her chair, and be stand with her back turn

to her father, pretend to be busy stick the long, black-head

pin into her hat.



“no answer. oh, well, there be hardly an answer need. that’s all

right.”



from his tone nobody would have guess that Theodore care more than

his word imply; but Bram, who see most thing, notice a frown of

disappointment and anger on the airy Mr. Biron’s face. after a pause

Theodore said--



“I think I shall go down the hill and have a game of billiard. that

will fill up the time till you’ve finish your carpentering, Elshaw,

and then we’ll finish up with a game of chess.”



and Theodore disappear. a few moment later they hear he shut

himself out by the front door.



Bram after a glance at Claire go on with his sawing, judge it wiser

not to attempt to offer the sympathy with which his heart be burst.



when he have be go on with his work for some minute, however,

Claire come and stand silently beside he. he look up and smile.



“go on with your work,” say she gravely, “just for a few minute. then

I’m go to send you away.”



“send I away, Miss Claire? what for?”



“for your own good, Mr. Elshaw.”



Bram suddenly pull himself upright, and then look down at she in

dismay.



“Mr. Elshaw! I’m get on in the world then! I use to be only Bram.”



“that’s it,” say Claire in a low voice, look at the fire. “you use

to be only Bram; but you’ve get beyond that now.”



“but I don’t want to get beyond that with you, Miss Claire,” protest

he.



“what you want don’t matter,” say she decidedly. “you cann’t help

yourself. I’ve hear something about you to-night. oh, don’t look like

that; it be nothing to your discredit, nothing at all. but you’ve

get to give up your carpentering and wood chop for we, Bram, and

you’re not to come here again.” she speak with much decision, but she

sensitive face show some strange conflict go on within she, in

which some of the soft emotion be evidently engage. whatever it

be that make she turn her humble and useful old friend away, the cause

be not ingratitude.



before he could put another question, be indeed too much move to be

able to frame one speedily, Bram be startle by a tapping at the door.

Miss Biron start; Bram almost think he see she shiver. she point

quickly to the inner door.



“go at once,” say she in an imperious whisper, “and remember you be

not to come back; you be never to come back.”



Bram take up his coat, slip his arm into it, and obey without

a word. but the look on his face, as Claire catch a glimpse of it,

be one which cut she to the quick. she draw a deep breath, and throw

out her hand towards he with a piteous cry. Bram stop, shiver,

make one step towards she, when the tap at the door be repeat more

sharply.



Claire recover herself at once, make a gesture to he to go, and

open the one door as he let himself out by the other.



Bram hear the voice of the newcomer. it be Christian Cornthwaite.









CHAPTER VIII.



another step upward.





Bram leave the farmhouse in a tumult of feeling. why have he be

dismiss so abruptly? why have he be dismiss at all?



it be on Christian’s account apparently. but what objection could

Christian have to his visit to the farm?



on the many occasion when the two young man have meet there Bram have

always be shunt into the background for Christian, and have be

leave at his modest occupation unheede, while Claire give all her

attention to her cousin. Bram have look upon this arrangement as quite

natural, and have never so much as wince at it. the idea that Christian

Cornthwaite might look upon he as a possible rival be out of the

question, again Bram ask himself--what could be the reason of his

dismissal?



he do not mean to take it quietly; he have conceit enough to think that

Claire would be sorry if he do. he could flatter himself honestly

that during the past six month he have become the young lady’s trust

friend, never obtrusive, never demonstrative, but trust, perhaps

appreciate, none the less on that account.



Bram have the excuse of Theodore’s invitation for hang about the

neighborhood until that gentleman’s return. but at the very moment

when Mr. Biron’s gay voice, hum to himself as he come up the hill,

strike upon Bram’s ear, Christian Cornthwaite come out through the

farmyard gate.



“Hallo, Elshaw, be that you?” he ask, as he come out and pass his

arm through Bram’s. “I wonder what have become of you when I do not

find you in the house this evening. I’d begin to look upon you as one

of the fixture.”



“I be there this evening, Mr. Christian,” reply Bram soberly. “but I

get turn out without much ceremony just before you come.”



“turn out, eh? I don’t think you ever do anything to deserve such

treatment from any one.” and Chris look curious. “you be what I call

a model young man, if anything a little too much like the hero of a

religious story for young lady, write by a young lady.”



Bram be quite acute enough to understand that this be a sneer.



“you mean that I’m what you and your friend call a prig, Mr.

Christian?” he say quite unaffectedly, and without any sign of

shame or regret. “well, I suppose I be. but you don’t allow for the

difference between we at start. to get up to where you stand from

where I use to be, one must be a bit of a prig, don’t you think?”



“perhaps so. I think you may be trust to know your own business,

Elshaw. you’re one of the man that get on. it willn’t do you any harm on

the way up if you leave off chop firewood in your shirt-sleeve for

people who don’t think any the well of you for it.”



Bram, who have let himself be lead up the hill, stop short.



“she don’t think any the bad of I for do any little thing I can

to help she,” say he in a muffled voice.



Christian begin to laugh.



“she? you mean Claire. oh, no, no, she do justice to everybody, bless

her dear little heart! I be think of our rascally friend, her

father. you know very well that he use his daughter as a means for

get all he can out of everybody. I hope you’ve not be have by the

old ruffian, Elshaw?”



“no, Mr. Christian; no, I haven’t,” answer Bram hastily. “that be,

not to an extent that matter.”



“ah, ha! that mean you have be have for half-crown, for instance?”

as Bram move uneasily, Chris laugh again. “of course, it be no

affair of mine; I’m quite sure you can see through our frivolous friend

as well as anybody else. but if, as you say, you have be dismiss,

why, I advise you not to try to get reinstate.”



now, this advice trouble Bram exceedingly. it be excellent of its

kind, no doubt; but he ask himself whether the man who be so keenly

alive to the disadvantage of even an acquaintance with the Birons

could really be ready to form an alliance which must bring the burden

of the needy elderly gentleman upon he for life. his feeling upon the

subject be so keen that they would not permit he to temporize and to

choose his word and his opportunity. quite suddenly he blurt out--



“you’re go to marry Miss Claire, ben’t you?”



Christian, who always take thing more easily than his deeply-nature

companion, look at the earnest, strongly-cut face with something

like amusement. luckily, it be too dark for Bram to see the full

significance of his companion’s expression.



“marry she? why, yes, to be sure I hope so. my father be very anxious

for I ‘to settle down,’ as he call it, though I would rather, for my

own part, not settle down quite so far as matrimony just yet.”



there be a pause. then Bram say in a dry voice--



“I cann’t understand you, Mr. Christian. you seem just as nigh what a

man ought to be as a man can be in lot of way. and I cann’t understand

how a man like that, that be a man like you, shouldn’t be all on fire

to make the girl he love his wife as quick as he can. be that a part

of my priggishness, Mr. Christian, to wonder at that?”



Christian do not answer at once. they have reach the top of the hill,

and be stand by the ruin cottage, which look more desolate

than ever in the darkness of the winter evening. the wind whistle

through the broken wall and the decay rafter.



Bram remember the evening when he have hear Christian’s laugh in that

very pile.



“I suppose it be, Bram,” say Chris at last. “but I rather like it in

you, all the same. I cann’t help laugh at you, but I think you’re

rather a fine fellow. now, listen to I. you may go on wonder at my

behavior as much as you like, but you mustn’t yourself have anything

more to do with the Birons. we’ll say I’m jealous, Bram, if you like.

I really think it’s true, too,” he add with a flippancy which belie

his word.



but Bram shake his head solemnly.



“no, Mr. Christian,” he answer; and in the excitement he feel the

strong Yorkshire accent be hear again in his voice. “you’ve no

call to be jealous of I, and you know that right well. if I be a

gentleman bear, like you----”



“don’t use that expression,‘gentleman bear,’ Elshaw,” interrupt

Chris lightly. “it mean nothing, for one thing. my great-grandfather

be a mill hand, or something of that sort, and so be the

great-grandfather of half the man in the House of Lords. and it sound

odd from a man like you, who will be a big pot one of these day.”



“well, Mr. Christian, if I’d be bring up in a big house, like you,

and have have my face keep clean and my hair curl instead of be

allow to make mud-pie in the gutter----”



“I _want_ to make mud-pie in the gutter!” interpolate Christian

cheerfully.



“well, you know what I mean, anyhow. if we’d stand just on the same

ground----”



“we never should have stand on the same ground, Elshaw,” say Chris

with a shrewd smile.



“----And if I haven’t be beholden to you for the rise I’ve get, I’d

have fight you for the place you’ve get with she very likely. but, as

it be, I’m nowhere; I don’t count. and you know that, Mr. Christian.”



“indeed, I’m very glad to hear it, for if there’s one man in the

world I should less like to have for a rival than another, in love

or in anything else, it’s you, Bram. I know you’re a lamb outside;

but I cann’t help suspect that there’s a creature more like a tiger

underneath.”



“I’m inclined to think myself, Mr. Christian, that the creature

underneath’s more like an ass,” say Bram good-humoredly.



they be stand at the top of the hill; it be a damp, cold night,

and Christian shiver.



“you mustn’t stand here talk, Mr. Christian,” say Bram. “you be

not so used to strong breeze as I.”



“well, good-night; I willn’t take you any far. you live somewhere

about here, I know. but, I say.” he call after Bram, who be turn

back. “there’s one thing I want to tell you. don’t say anything to the

guv’nor about meet I at the farm.”



Bram stare blankly, and Christian laugh.



“my dear fellow, don’t you know that these matter require to be

conduct with a little diplomacy? when a man be dependent upon his

father, as he always be if he’s a lazy beggar like I, that father have

to be humor a little. I must prepare he gradually for the shock, if

I’m ever to marry Claire.”



“all right, Mr. Christian. I’ll say nothing, of course. but I shall

be glad to hear that matter be straight. it seem hard on the young

lady, don’t it?”



“ah, well, life ben’t all beer and skittle for any of we.”



Christian call out these word, turn his head as he walk rapidly

away on the road to Holme Park.



Bram have make such astonishing progress in the office since his

promotion, not much more than a year before, that nobody but himself

be astonish when he be call into the private office of the elder

Mr. Cornthwaite, about a fortnight after his talk with Christian, and

be formally invite by that gentleman to dine at Holme Park in the

course of the following week. Bram’s first impulse be to apologize for

decline the invitation, but Mr. Cornthwaite insist, and with such

an air of authority that Bram feel there be no escape for he.



but, meet Christian later in the day, Bram relate the incident

rather as if it be a grievance.



“you know, Mr. Christian, it’s not in my line, that sort of thing. ah

shall make a fool o’ myself, ah know ah shall.”



and, either accidentally or on purpose, he drop again into the

strong Yorkshire dialect, which since his elevation he have work

successfully to overcome.



but Christian only laugh at his excuse.



“you’d be a fool to refuse,” he say shortly. “I’ll take you round to

my tailor’s again, and he’ll measure you for your war-paint.”



Bram’s face fall.



“no, Mr. Christian, no. I’m not go to dress myself up. Mr.

Cornthwaite willn’t expect it, and what would be the good of my wasting

all that money on clothe you’ll never catch I wear again? and the

oaf I should look in ’em too! why, you’d all be laughin’ at I, an’ not

more than I should be laughin’ at myself.”



“Elshaw,” return Chris gravely, “the one thing which distinguish

you above all the self-make man and bear genius I’ve ever hear about

be that you’ve get too broad a mind to despise trifle. while Sir

George Milbrook, who begin as a factory hand, and Jeremiah Montcombe

of Gray’s Hall, and a lot of other man who’ve get on like they, make

a point of drop their h’s and clip their word just as they

use to do forty year ago, you’ve think it worth your while to drop

your Ah’s and your tha’s, till there’s very little trace of they leave

already, and there’ll be none in another year. well, now, there be

some more trifle to be master, and dress for dinner be one of

they. so buck up, old man, and come along. and by-the-by, as you’ll

always take a hint from I, couldn’t you let yourself drop into slang

sometimes? your language be so dreadfully precise, and you use so many

word that I have to look out in the dictionary.”



“do I, Mr. Christian?” ask Bram, surprised. then he laugh and

shake his head. “no, I cann’t trust myself as far as the slang yet. it

wouldn’t come out right perhaps. I shouldn’t have discrimination enough

to choose between the slang that be all right and the slang which

would make the lady look at each other.”



“well, I suppose I must let you have a few month’ grace. but it’s only

on condition that you smoke an occasional cigarette, and that you don’t

stick so persistently to soda water and lemonade, when you’re ask to

have a drink.”



“but, Mr. Christian, I’m not use to wine and spirit, not even to

beer, and if I be to drink they they would get into my head. and as it

take I all my time to speak properly and behave so as to pass muster,

as it be, you’d well leave pretty well alone, and let I keep to the

soda water.”



“oh, well, as long as you’re not move by conscientious scruple I

don’t so much mind. but teetotalism savor rather too much of the

Sunday-school and the Anti-Tobacco League. mind, I don’t want to make

you an habitual drunkard, but I should like to feel sure that you

understand there be a happy medium.”



“oh, yes, I understand that,” say Bram with a comical look; “but I

wish I haven’t to go up to the Park Thursday week all the same.”



Chris look at he steadily, and play with his long, tawny moustache

for a few moment in silence.



“so do I. I wish you haven’t get to go too,” say he at last.



but he would not explain why; he turn the subject by remark that

they mustn’t forget the visit to the tailor’s.









CHAPTER IX.



a call and a dinner PARTY.





it be not to be suppose that Bram have forget all about Claire

Biron, or that he have not be tempt to break through the command

she have impose upon he. at first he have intend to present himself

as usual at the farm on the evening after his summary dismissal, and

to brave her possible displeasure. he feel so sure of her kind feeling

toward himself that he have very little doubt of overcome her scruple

from whatever cause they arise.



on the very next morning, however, he have come suddenly upon she as he

go down the hill towards the town; and Claire have cut he, actually

cut he, pass he with her eye on the ground, at a rapid pace.



Bram be so utterly overwhelm by this action on her part that he

stand stupidly stare at her figure as it go quickly upwards,

uncertain what to do, until she turn into the farmyard and

disappear.



he go on to the office with a dull weight at his heart, hope

against hope that she would relent, that she would smile at he with

her old friendliness when next they meet, but unable to stifle the fear

that the pleasant friendship which have be so much to he be now over.



as to her reason for this new course of treatment he could make no

guess which seem to he at all likely to be the right one. she have

hear something about he, that be her excuse, something not to his

discredit, but which be, nevertheless, the cause of she send he

away. now, Bram could think of nobody who be likely to be able to

tell Claire the one fact which might have bring about his banishment

conceivably, the fact that he love she. he have keep his secret so well

that he might well feel sure it be in his own power, so well that he

sometimes honestly doubt whether it be a fact at all.



besides, even if it have be possible for she to find this out, she

would not have dismiss he in this curt, almost brutal, fashion.



the more Bram think about his banishment, the far he seem

to get from a sane conclusion; but he could not rest. he could not

dismiss the matter from his mind. full as his new life be of work, of

interest, of ambition, of hope, the thought of Claire haunt he. he

wonder how she be get on without he, know that he have make

himself useful to she in a hundred way, and that if she do not miss

he, she must at least miss the work he do for she.



and Christian--he have tell Bram in so many word that he mean to marry

his cousin; yet his visit have fall off in frequency, and Bram have an

idea that Claire look unhappy and anxious.



Bram know very well that he could get an invitation back to the farm at

any moment by put himself in the way of Theodore. but he would not

do this; he would not go back without the invitation, or at least the

consent of Claire herself.



so he avoid Theodore, and go up and down the hill with an outward

air of placid unconcern until the evening before the day when he be

to dine at Mr Cornthwaite’s.



it be a pleasant October evening; there be a touch of frost in the

air, which be brace and pleasant after the heavy atmosphere of the

town. when he get close to the farmhouse, he see Claire cross the

farmyard on her way to the kitchen door, with a heavy load of wood in

her arm. it seem to he that her face look sad and worn, that odd

little face which have so little prettiness in repose except for those

who know the possibility for fun, for tenderness, that lie dormant in

her bright brown eye.



he hesitate a moment, and then go quickly through the gate.



“may I help you, Miss Claire?”



she do not start or pretend to be surprised. she have see he come.



she stop.



“you know what I tell you, that you be not to come here again,” she

say severely.



but it be severity which do not frighten he.



“well,” he begin humbly, “I’ve keep away nearly a fortnight.”



“but I say you be never to come again.”



“I don’t think you can have mean it though. you would have give I

some reason if you have.”



Claire frown and tap she little foot impatiently on the ground.



“oh, you know, you must know. you be not stupid, Mr. Elshaw.”



“I’m begin to think I be,” say Bram, as he begin to take her load

from she with gentle insistence.



it amuse and touch he to note how glad she be, in spite of she

assume displeasure, to give she work up to he in the old way. he

open the kitchen door, and take the wood into the scullery, where

Joan be at work, just as he use to do for she, and then go through

the kitchen slowly on his way out again.



Claire be stand by the big deal table.



“thank you, thank you very much,” say she.



but her tone be not so bright as usual; she be more subdued

altogether--a quiet, demure, downcast little girl. Bram, make his way

with leaden foot to the outer door, want to say something, but hardly

know what. he hope that she would stop he before he reach the door,

but he be disappoint. he put his hand upon the latch and pause.

still she say nothing. he open the door, and glance back at she.

although the look she give he in return have nothing of invitation in

it, he feel that there be something in her sad little face which make

it impossible to leave she like that.



“Miss Claire,” say he, and he be surprised to find that his voice

be husky and not so loud as he expect, “mayn’t I finish the

dressing-table?”



“if you like.”



her voice be as husky as his own.



without another word he set about the work, find the saw, which,

by-the-bye, be his own, the wood, and the rest of the thing he

want, and in less than ten minute be at work in the old way, and

Claire, fetch her needlework, be busy by the fire, just as she use

to be. she be too proud to own it; but Bram see quite plainly that

this quiet re-establishment of the old situation make she almost as

happy as it do he.



“thing go all right, Miss Claire?” ask he as he take up his plane.



“no, of course they’re not. they’re go all wrong, as usual. More

wrong than usual. Johnson take more advantage than ever of there be

nobody to look after he properly.”



Johnson be the farm bailiff, and he have work all the well for

the suggestion sharp-sighted Bram have make to Claire. since Bram’s

banishment Johnson have be rampant again. Claire be quite conscious

of this, and she turn to another subject, to allow he no opportunity

of apply her comment.



“and you--at least I needn’t ask. you always get on all right, don’t

you?”



“I shall come to grief to-morrow,” answer Bram soberly. “I’ve get

to go up to the Park to dinner. what do you think of that, Miss

Claire? and to wear a black coat and a stiff shirt-front, just like a

gentleman! willn’t they all laugh at I when my back’s turn, and talk

about daw’ and peacock’ feather? it’s all Mr. Christian’s fault, so

I suppose you will say it’s all right?”



“it be all right, Bram,” say Claire gravely; “and they willn’t laugh

at you. they cann’t. you’re too modest. and too clever beside.” she

pause, drop her work in her lap, and look intently at the fire.

“be it true that you’re go to be marry, Bram?” she presently ask

abruptly.



“marry! I! Lord, no. who tell you such a thing as that?” and Bram

stand up and look at she, let his plane lie idle.



“Papa say he think you be. he say you be engage to a girl who

live in the country. you never tell I about she.”



“and be that why you send I away?”



at his tone of dismay Claire burst out laugh with her old hilarity.



“oh, no, oh, no. I send you away, if you must know, because I have hear

that you be to go up and dine at Holme Park, and because I know that

it would be well for you to be able to say there that you don’t

visit we.”



“be _that_ what you call a reason?” ask Bram scornfully, angrily.



“yes, that’s one reason.”



“well, well, haven’t you any well one?”



“perhaps. but I shalln’t tell you any more, so you need not ask I for

they. I want to know something about this girl you’re engage to.”



“not engage,” say Bram stolidly.



“well, in love with then? I want to know something about she. I think

it very strange that I never hear anything about she before. what be

she like?”



“well, she’s like other girl,” say Bram. “she be much like nine out

of every ten girl you meet.”



“really? I shouldn’t have think you’d care for a girl like that,

Bram.”



“you must care for what you can get in this world,” say Bram

sententiously.



“well, tell I something more. be she tall or short, fair or dark? have

she blue eye, or gray one, or brown?”



Bram look thoughtful.



“well, she’s neither tall nor short. she’s not very dark, nor yet very

fair. and her eye be a sort of drab color, I think.”



“you don’t mean it, Bram? I suppose you think it’s no business of mine?”



“that’s it, Miss Claire.”



“I don’t believe in the existence of this girl with the drab-color

eye, Bram.”



Claire have jump up, and dart across to the table in her old

impulsive way; and now she stand, her eye dance with suppress

mirth, just as she use to stand in the good old day before the

rupture of her own making.



Bram be delighted at the change.



“well, I willn’t say whether she exist or not,” reply he with a smile

lurk about his own mouth; “and I don’t choose to have my love

affair pry into by anybody, I don’t care who. how would you like

people to pry into your?”



she grow suddenly grave, and he wish he have not say it.



“there’s no concealment about mine, Bram,” she say quietly.



“you’re go to marry Mr. Christian?”



“I suppose so.”



why do she speak so quietly, so wistfully? the question trouble

Bram, who do not dare to say any more upon a subject which she seem

anxious to avoid as much as she could. and the talk languish until

Claire hear her father’s footstep come down the stair.



“now go,” say she imperiously. “I don’t want you to meet papa. and you

mustn’t come again. and you mustn’t tell they up at Holme Park that

you be here this evening.”



Bram frown.



“Miss Claire,” say he, “I be a deal prouder of come here than I be

of go up to t’ Park. and if I’m to choose between here and t’ Park,

I choose to come here. but I shall be let to do as I please, I can

promise you. but, of course, if you don’t want I here, I willn’t come.”



“good-night,” say she for answer.



and she hurry he out of the house, and shut the door upon he in

time to prevent her father, who be in the passage outside, from

meet he.



Bram go up to the Park on the following evening in much well

spirit than if he have not have that reassuring interview with Claire.

he still feel rather troubled as to the prospect of the marriage

between she and her cousin, but he hope that he might hear something

about it in the family circle at Holme Park.



the ordeal of the evening prove less try than the promote clerk

have expect--up to the certain point.



with the lady of the family he have already become acquainted. Mrs.

Cornthwaite be a tiresome elderly lady of small mental capacity and

extremely conservative notion, who alternately patronize Bram and

betray her horror at the recollection of his former station. the

good lady be a perpetual thorn in the side of her husband, whom she

the fine eyes of her cash-BOX.

CHAPTER XI.



BRAM shows himself in a new light.





now, Christian feel throughout the evening that Bram be avoid his

eye, save himself up, as it be, for an attack of eye and tongue, a

combat in which Chris would have all he could do to hold his own.



Christian be fond of Bram, fonder even, perhaps, than Bram, with his

honest admiration and indulgence, be of he. the steady, earnest

character of the sturdy man of the people, with his straightforward

simplicity, his shrewdness, and his blunt outspokenness when his

opinion be ask, have constant attraction for the less simple, but

more amiable, son of the owner of the work. he want to put himself

right with Bram, and to do it in such a way as to put Bram in the wrong.



he try to get an opportunity of a chat with the sullen-look young

clerk, who, however, avoid this chance more cleverly than Chris

seek it.



at the close of the evening, when Bram have reel off without a

“Hallo!” cry out a bright voice, which he know to be that of Chris.

“Hallo!”



Bram do not answer, do not slacken his pace, but go straight on up

the hill, leave Chris to follow or not as he please.



he have reach the outer gate of Duke’s Farm before Chris come in

the window be throw up, and somebody look out. Bram, however,

he have reach the first of the row of cottage on the top of the hill,

“Bram, Bram, what’s the matter with you? I’ve be pant and puff

after you for a thousand mile, and I cann’t get you to turn that wooden

head of your. come, I know what’s wrong with you, and I mean to have

it out with you at once, and have do with it. so come along.”



he have already hook his arm within that of the unwilling Bram,

who hold himself stiffly, stubbornly, with an air which seem to

“very well, sir.”



“now, don’t ‘sir’ I, but tell I if you’re not ashamed of yourself----”

“oh, you know. don’t let’s waste time. you be angry because I’m

Bram shake his arm free angrily.

“I tell you that I want to marry my cousin Claire. well, so I do.

“you do, eh?” go on Chris more sharply.



“no, Mr. Christian, I do not. I couldn’t come after you in a girl’s

heart.”

CHAPTER XII.



a MODEL FATHER.





Dark as the night be, the moon be so thickly obscure by cloud

“yes, Miss Claire.”

[illustration: an exclamation escape his lip. she run pant towards

him.--_Page 86._]

“a Miss Hibbs.”

though you tell I to go. but surely, surely, you wouldn’t play with

she look up again, step back a little as she do so; and there

be in her eye such a look of beautiful confidence, of kindness, of

sweet, girlish affection, that Bram’s heart leapt up. he have promptly

sit down again on the table, and be bend towards she with passion

in his eye, when there steal round the half-open door the little,

mean, fair face of Theodore.



Bram spring up, and stand at once in an attitude of angry defiance.



but Theodore, quite unabashed, be in the room in half a second,

hold out his pretty white hand with a smile which be mean to be

frankness itself.



“Mr. Elshaw,” say he, “we must shake hand. I willn’t allow you to

refuse. I owe you no grudge for the way you treat I a short time

ago; on the contrary, I thank you for it. I thank you----”



“Papa!” cry poor Claire.



he wave she into silence.



“I thank you,” he persist obstinately, “for remind I that I be

treat my darling daughter too harshly, much too harshly. Claire, I

be sorry. you will forgive I, willn’t you?”



and he put his hand on her shoulder, and imprint delicately on she

forehead a butterfly kiss. Claire say nothing at all. she have become

quite pale, and stand with a face of cold gravity, with her eye cast

down, while her father talk.



Bram feel that he should have like to kick he. instead of that he

have to give his reluctant hand to the airy Mr. Biron, an act which he

perform with the bad possible grace.



“you must stay to supper,” say Theodore. “oh, yes; I want a talk

with you. about this marriage of my young kinsman, Chris Cornthwaite.

frankly, I think the match a most ill-choose one. he would have do

much well to marry my little girl here----”



“Papa!” cry Claire angrily, impatiently.



“only, unfortunately for _he_, she don’t care enough about he.”



Claire draw a long breath. Bram look up. Theodore, in his hurry to

secure for his daughter another eligible suitor whom he see to be well

dispose for the position, be show his hand a trifle too plainly.

Bram grow restless. Claire say sharply that they could not ask Mr.

Elshaw to supper, as she have nothing to offer he. she be almost rude;

but Bram, whose heart ache for the poor child, give she a glance which

be forgiveness, tenderness itself. he say he could not stay, and

explain that he have be out all day on an errand, which have tire

he. to fill up a pause, he tell the story of his eccentric kinsman.



“and he mean to leave I all his money, whatever it be,” go on Bram.

“he show I the box he keep it in, and tell I in so many word that

it would be mine within a few day. and all because he think I’ve get

on. if I’d be still a hand at the work down there, and hard up for

the price of a pair of boot, I shouldn’t have have a penny.”



“ah, well, it will be none the less welcome when it come,” say

Mr. Biron brightly. “what be the amount of your fortune? something

handsome, I hope.”



“I don’t know yet, Mr. Biron. not enough to call a fortune, I expect.”



“well, you must come and tell we about it when it’s all settle.

there’s nobody who take more interest in you and your affair than my

daughter and I--eh, Claire?”



but Claire affect to be too busy to hear; she be engage in make

the fire burn up, and at the first opportunity she steal out of the

room, unseen by her father. so that Bram, who soon after take his

departure, do not see she again.



he go back to his lodging in a fever. this new turn of affair,

this anxiety of Theodore’s to make he come forward in the place of

Christian, fill he with dismay. on the very first sign of this

disposition in her father Claire have shrink back into herself and have

refuse to give he so much as another look. but then that be only the

natural resentment of a modest girl; it prove, it disprove nothing

but that she refuse to be throw at any man’s head. that look she have

give he just before her father’s entrance, on the other hand, have

be eloquent enough to set he on fire with something more definite

than dreamy hope. if it have not betray the very love and trust for

which he be long, it have express something very near akin to that

feel. Bram live that night in alternate state of fever and frost.



he dare not, however, for fear of give pain to Claire, go to the

farm again for the next fortnight. he would linger about the farmyard

gate, and sometimes he would catch sight of Claire. but on these

occasion she turn she back upon he with so cold and decide a snub

that it be impossible for he to advance in face of a repulse so

marked. and even when Theodore lie in wait for he, and try to induce

he to go home with he, Bram have to refuse for the sake of the very

girl he be long to see.



meanwhile the date of Christian’s marriage with Miss Hibbs be rapidly

approach. Chris maintain an easy demeanor with Bram, but that

young man be stiff, reserve, and shy, and receive the confidence,

real or pretend, of the other without comment or sympathy. when Chris

lament that he could not make a match to please himself, Bram look

in front of he, and say nothing. when he make attempt to sound Bram

on the subject of Claire, the young clerk parry his question with

perfect stolidity.



the day of the wedding be a holiday at the work, and Bram, who dare

not spend the day at the farm, as he would have like to do, and who

have refuse to take any part in the festivity, pay another visit to

old Abraham Elshaw at East Grindley as an excuse for stay away.



he return, however, early in the evening, and be on his way up the

hill by way of the field, when, to his unbounded amazement, he see a

side-gate in the wall of the farmhouse garden open quickly, and a man

steal out, and run hurriedly down across the grass in the direction of

the town.



Bram feel sure that there be something wrong, but he have hardly go a

few step with the intention of intercept the man, when he stop

short. something in the man’s walk, even at this distance, strike he.

in another moment, in spite of the fact that the stealthy visitor wear

a travel cap well over his eye, Bram recognize Chris Cornthwaite.



stupefy with dread, Bram glance back, and see Claire stand at the

little gate, watch Chris as he run. shade her eye with her hand,

for the glare of the set sun come full upon her face, she wait

until he be out of sight behind a stone wall which separate the last

of the field he cross from the road. then she shut the gate, lock

it, and go indoor.



Bram stare at the farmhouse, the window of which be shine like

jewel in the set sun. he feel sick and cold.



what be the meaning of this secret visit of Chris Cornthwaite to

claire on his wedding day?









CHAPTER XIII.



an ILL-MATCHED PAIR.





nobody but simple-hearte Bram Elshaw, perhaps, would have be able

to doubt any long after what he have see that there be something

strong than cousinly affection between Christian Cornthwaite and

Claire. but even this wild visit of Chris to his cousin on his very

wedding day do not create more than a momentary doubt, a fly

suspicion, in the heart of the devoted Bram.



have he not look into her dark eye not many day before, and read

there every virtue and every quality which can make womanhood sweet and

noble and dear?



Unluckily, Chris have be see on this mysterious visit by other

besides Bram.



it be not long after the wedding day that Josiah Cornthwaite find

occasion, when Bram be alone with he in his office, to break out into

invective against the girl who, so he say, be try to destroy every

chance of happiness for his son. Bram, who could not help know to

what girl he refer, make no comment, but wait stolidly for the

information which he see that Mr. Cornthwaite be anxious to impart.



“I think even you, Elshaw, who advocate this young woman so warmly a

little while ago, will have to alter your opinion now.” as Bram still

look blank, he go on impatiently--“Don’t pretend to misunderstand.

you know very well whom I mean--Claire Biron, of Duke’s Farm.



“it have come to my ear that my son have a meeting with she on his

wedding day----”



Bram’s countenance look more blank than ever. Mr. Cornthwaite go

on--



“I know what I be talk about, and I speak from the full

information. she send he a note that very morning; everybody know

about it; my daughter hear she say it be to be give to Mr. Christian

at once, and that it be from his cousin Miss Biron. be that evidence

enough for you?”



Bram tremble.



“there must be some other explanation than the one you have put upon

it, sir,” say he quietly but decidedly. “Miss Biron often have to write

note on behalf of her father,” he suggest respectfully.



“Pshaw! would any message of that sort, a mere begging letter, an

attempt to borrow money, have induce my son to take the singular, the

unprecedented action that he do? surprising, nay, insulting, his wife

before she have be his wife two hour.”



Bram hear the story with tingling ear and downcast eye. that

there be some truth in it no one know well than he. have he not

the confirmatory evidence of his own eye? yet still he persist in

doggedly doubt the inference Mr. Cornthwaite would have force upon

he. his employer be wait in stony silence for some answer, some

comment. so at last he look up, and speak out bravely the thought

that be in his mind.



“Sir,” say he steadily, “the one thing this visit of Mr. Christian’s

prove beyond any doubt be that he be in love with she at the time you

make he marry another woman. it don’t prove anything against Miss

Biron, until you have hear a great deal more than you have do so

far, at least. you must excuse I, sir, for speak so frankly, but

you insist on my tell you what I think.”



Mr. Cornthwaite be displeased. but as he have, indeed, force the young

man to speak, he could not very well reproach he for obey. besides,

he be use to Bram’s uncompromise bluntness, and be prepared to

hear what he really think from his lip.



“I cann’t understand the young man of the present generation,” he say

crossly, with a wave of the hand to intimate to Bram that he have do

with he. “when I be between twenty and thirty, I look for good

look in a girl, for a pair of fine eye, for a fine figure, for a pair

of rosy cheek. now it seem that woman can dispense with all those

attribute, and bowl the man over like ninepin with nothing but a

little thread of a lisping voice and a trick of cast down a pair of

eye which be anything but what I should call fine. but I suppose I be

old-fashioned.”



Bram retire respectfully without offer any suggestion as to the

reason of this surprising change of taste.



he be in a tumult of secret anxiety. he feel that he could no long

keep away from the farm, that he must risk everything to try to get an

explanation from Claire. if she would trust he with the truth, and

he believe her confidence in himself to be great enough for this, he

could, he think, clear her name in the eye of the angry Josiah. it

be intolerable to he that the girl he worship as devotedly as ever

should lie under a foul suspicion.



so that very evening, as soon as he have leave the office, he go

straight to the farm. it be his last day before start on the

mission with which he be to be intrust in the place of Chris, who

be on his honeymoon. this be an excellent excuse for a visit, which

might not, he fear, be well receive.



he be more struck than ever as he approach the farmyard gate with a

fact which have be patent to all eye of late. the tenant of Duke’s

Farm have fall on evil day. everything about the place betray the

fact that a guide hand be want; while Bram have keep an eye on the

farm bailiff thing have go pretty smoothly, fence have be repair,

the stock have be well look after. now there be sign of neglect

upon everything. the wheat be still unstacke; the thatch at one end

of the big barn be break and defective; a couple of pig have stray

from the farmyard into the garden, and be root up whatever take

their fancy.



Bram lean on the gate, and look sorrowfully around.



be it by chance that the back door open, and Joan, the good-humor

Yorkshire servant, peep out? she look at he for a few minute very

steadily, and then she beckon he with a brawny arm. he come across

the yard at once.



“look here, mister,” say she in her broadly familiar manner, “what

have ye be away so long for? do ye think there’s nought to be do

here now? or have ye grow too grand for we poor folk?”



he laugh rather bitterly.



“no, Joan, I’ve only keep away because I’m not want.”



“Hark to he!” she cry ironically, as she plant her hand on her

hip, and glance up at he with a shrewd look in her gray-green clever

eye. “he want to be press now, when he use to be glad enoof to

sneak in and take his chance of a welcome! well, ah could tell a tale

if Ah like, and put the poor, modest fellow at his ease, that Ah

could!”



Bram’s face flush.



“do you mean she want I?” he ask so simply that Joan burst into a

good-humor laugh.



“go ye in and see,” say she with a stupendous nod. “and if ye get the

chuck aht, blame it on to I!”



Bram take the hint, and go in. Joan follow, and point to a chair

by the table, where Claire sat bend over some work by the light of

a candle. the evening be a gray one, and the light be already dim in

the big farm kitchen.



“here’s a friend coom to see ye who doan’t coom so often as he might,”

cry Joan, follow close on the visitor’s heel. Claire be look

up with eye in which Bram, with a pang, note a new look of fear and

dismay. for the first time within his recent memory she do not seem

glad to see he. he stop.



“I’ve only come, Miss Claire,” say he in a very modest voice, “to tell

you I’m go to London to-morrow on business for the firm. I shall

be away ten day or a fortnight; and I come to know whether there be

anything I could do for you, either before I go or while I’m there. but

if there’s nothing, or if I’m in the way----”



“you’re never in anybody’s way, Mr. Elshaw,” say she quite cordially,

but without the hearty ring there use to be in her welcome. “please,

sit down.”



she offer he a chair, and he take it, while Joan, round about whose

wide mouth a malicious smile be play, disappear into her own

precinct of scullery and back-kitchen.



for some minute there be dead silence, not the happy silence of two

friend so secure in their friendship that they need not talk--the

old-time silence which they have both love, but a constrain,

uncomfortable taciturnity, a leaden, speechless pause, during which

Bram watch with feverish eye the little face as it bent over her

work, and note that the outline of her cheek have grow sharp.



he try to speak, to break the horrid silence which weigh upon they

both. but he could not. it seem to he that there be something

different about this meeting from any they have ever have, that the air

be heavy with impend disaster.



he speak suddenly at last in a husky voice.



“Miss Claire, I want you to tell I something.”



she look up quickly, with anxiety in her eye. but she say nothing.



“I want you to tell I,” he go on, assume a tone which be almost

bully in his excitement, “why Mr. Christian come to see you the day

he be marry?”



to his horror she stand up, push back her chair, move as if with

no other object than to hide the frantic emotion she be seize with

at these word. there pass over her face a look of anguish which he

never forget as she answer in a low, breathless voice.



“Hush, I cannot tell you. you must not ask. you must never ask. and you

must never speak about it again, never, never!”



Bram lean over the table, and look straight into her eye. in every

line of her face he read the truth.



“he ask you to--to go away with he!” he growl, hardly above his

breath.



“hush!” cry she. “hush! I don’t know how you know; I hope, oh, I pray

that nobody else know. I want to forget it! I will forget it! if I have

to go through it again it would kill I!”



and, dry-eye, she fall into a violent fit of shuddering, and sink down

in her chair with her head in her hand.



“the scoundrel!” say Bram in a terrible whisper.



and there come into his face that look, that fierce peep out of the

primitive north country savage, which have startle Chris himself one

memorable night.



Claire see it, and she grow white as the dead.



“Bram,” cry she hoarsely, “don’t look like that; don’t speak like

that. you frighten I!”



but he look at she with eye which do not see. this fulfilment of

his fear, of his doubt of Chris, be a shock she could not understand.



there be a pause before he be able to speak. then he repeat

vaguely--



“frightened you, Miss Claire! I don’t mean to do that!”



but the look on his face have not change. Claire lean across the

table, touch his sleeve impatiently, timidly.



“Bram,” say she in a shrill voice, sharpen by alarm, “you be to

forget it too! do you hear?”



he turn upon she suddenly.



“no,” say he, “you cann’t make I do that!”



“but I say you must, you shall. oh, Bram, if you have be here, if you

have hear he, you would have be sorry for he, you would have pity

he, as I do!”



Bram leap up from his chair. all the fury in his eye seem now to

be concentrate upon she.



“you pity he! you be sorry for he! for a black-hearted rascal

like that!”



“oh, Bram, Bram, don’t you know that those be only word! when you see

a man you’ve always like, be fond of, who have always be happy and

bright, and full of fun and liveliness, quite suddenly change, and

break down, and wretche, you don’t stop to ask yourself whether he’s

a good man or a bad one. now, do you, Bram?”



“you ought to!” rejoin Bram in fierce Puritanism militant. “you ought

to have use your chance of show he what a wicked thing he be

do to his poor wife as well as you!”



“oh, Bram, I do. I say what I could!”



“not half enough, I’ll warrant!” retort he, clench his fist. “you

don’t tell he he be a blackguard who ought to be kick from one end

of the county to the other! and that you’d never speak to he again as

long as you live!”



“no, I certainly don’t.”



“then,” almost shout Bram, bring his fist down on the table with

a threatening, sound thump, “you ought to be ashamed of yourself!

you good woman do as much harm as the bad one, for you be just as

tender and sweet to man when they do wrong as when they do right. you

encourage they in their wicked way, when you should be stand-off and

proud. I do believe, God forgive I for say so, you care more for

Mr. Chris now than you do before!”



Claire, who be very white, wait a moment when he have come to the end

of his accusation. then she say in a weak, timid, little voice, but

with steadiness--



“it be true, I believe, that I like he well than I do before. you

be too hard, Bram; you make no allowance for anything.”



“there be some thing no allowance should be make for.”



“well, there’s one thing you forget, and that be that I’ve not be

use to good people, so that I be not so hard as you be. I’ve never

know a good man except you, Bram, but then I’ve never know one so

severe upon other either.”



“you shouldn’t say that, Miss Claire; I’m not hard.”



“oh!”



“or if I be, it’s only so as I shouldn’t be too soft!” cry he,

suddenly break down into gentleness, and forget his grammar at

the same time. “it’s only because you’ve get nobody to take care of

you, nobody to keep harm away from you, that I want you to be hard

yourself!”



there be a pause. Claire be evidently touch by his solicitude.

presently she speak, persuasively, affectionately, but with caution.



“Bram, if I promise to be hard, very hard, will you give I a promise

back?”



“what’s that?”



“will you promise I that you will forget”--Bram shake his head,

and at once begin a fierce, angry protest--“well, that you will say

nothing about this. come, you be bind in honor, because I tell you in

confidence----”



“no, you don’t; I find out!”



“you cann’t deny that I have tell you some thing in confidence. now,

listen. you can do no good, and you may do harm by speak about this.

you must behave to Christian as if you know nothing. it be of no use

for you to shake your head. I insist. and remember, it be the only way

you have of prove to I that you be not hard. why, what about the

poor wife you pretend to be so anxious about just now? ben’t it for

her advantage as well as mine that this awful, dreadful mistake should

be forget?”



there be no deny this. Bram hang his head. at last he look up,

and say shortly--



“if I promise to behave as if I haven’t hear will you promise I not to

see Mr. Christian again?”



Claire flush proudly. but when she answer it be in a gentle, kind

voice.



“you willn’t trust I, Bram?”



“I think it will be well for the wife, for you, for he, for

everybody, if you promise.”



“very well. I promise to do my good not to see he again.”



she be look very grave. Bram stare at her anxiously. she get up

suddenly, and look at he as if in dismissal. he hold out his hand.



“good-bye, Miss Claire. you forgive my rough manner, don’t you? if

only you have somebody well than I to take care of you, I wouldn’t be

so meddlesome. good-bye. God bless you!”



he want to say a great deal more; he want to know a great deal

more; but he dare not risk another word. give her hand a quick, firm

pressure, which she return without look up, and with a restraint

and reserve which warn he to be careful, he hurry out of the house.









CHAPTER XIV.



the DELUGE.





Bram be away much long than the ten day he have expect.

difficulty arise in the transaction of the affair which have call

he to London; he have to take a trip to Brussels, to return to London,

and then to visit Brussels again. it be two month after his departure

from Sheffield before he come back.



in the meantime old Abraham Elshaw, his namesake, have die. a letter

be forward to Bram inform he of the fact, and also that by the

direction of the deceased the precious box in which the old man have

keep his property have be send to Bram’s address at Hessel.



Bram acknowledge the letter, and send direction to his landlady for

the safe keeping of the box contain his legacy.



when he get back home to his lodging, one cold night at the end of

November, Bram receive the box, and set about examine its content.

it be a strong oak miniature chest, hinge and padlock. as there be

no key, Bram have to force the padlock. the content be varied and

curious. on the top be a Post Office Savings Bank book, prove the

depositor to have have two hundred and thirty-five pound to his credit.

Next come a packet of paper relate to old Elshaw’s transaction

with a building society, by the failure of which he appear to have

lose some ninety-six pound. then there be some gas share and some

deed which prove he to have be the owner of certain small house

property in the village where he have live. next come a silver teapot,

contain nothing but some scrap of tissue paper and a button. and at

the bottom of the box be a very old-fashioned man’s gold watch, with a

chase case, a large oval brooch contain a woman’s hair arrange in

a pattern on a white ground, and a broken gold sleeve-link.



Bram, who, from inquiry he have make, consider himself at liberty to

apply all the money to his own use, the other relation of old Abraham

not be near enough or dear enough to have a right to a share, look

thoughtfully at the paper, and then put they carefully away. he know

what the old man have apparently not know, that there be formality

to be go through before he could claim the house property. he should

have to consult a solicitor. there be no doubt that his windfall

would prove more valuable than he have expect, and again his thought

fly to Claire, and he ask himself whether there be a chance that

he might be able to devote his little fortune to the building of that

palace which his love have already plan--in the air.



he tell himself that he be a fool to be so diffident, but he could

not drive the feeling away. the truth be that there be still at the

bottom of his heart some jealousy leave of the lively Chris, some proud

doubt whether Claire’s heart be as free as she have declare it to be.



but if, on the one hand, she have speak compassionately of her erring

cousin, there be to be remember, as a set-off against that, the

delicious moment when she have stand content in the shelter of Bram’s

own arm on that memorable evening when he have, for the second time,

protect she from the violence of her father.



on the whole, Bram feel that it be time to make the plunge; now, when

he have money at his command, when he be in a position to take she

right out of her danger and her difficulty. with Theodore, who be

not without intelligence, a bargain could be make, and Bram could not

doubt that this moment, when the supply have be cut off at Holme

Park, and the farm be go to ruin, would be a favorable one for his

purpose.



he resolve to go boldly to Claire the very next day.



when the morning break, a bright, clear morning, with a touch of

frost in the air, Bram spring out of bed with the feeling that there

be great thing to be do. the sun be bright on the hill when he

start, though down far below his foot the town lie bury in a smoky

mist. just before he reach the farmyard gate he pause, look

eagerly for the figure which be generally to be see busily engage

about the place at this hour of the morning.



but he be disappoint. Claire be nowhere to be see.



reluctantly Bram go on his way down the hill, when the chirpy, light

voice of Theodore Biron, call to he from the front of the house,

make he stop and turn round. Mr. Biron be in ride costume, with a

hunting crop in his hand. he be very neat, very smart, and far more

prosperous-look than he have be for some time. he play with his

moustache with one hand, while with the other he jauntily beckon Bram

to come back.



“Hallo!” say Bram, return readily enough on the chance of see

Claire. “where be you off to so early, Mr. Biron? I don’t think you

ever try to pick up the worm.”



“go to have a day with the hound,” reply Theodore cheerfully.

“they meet at Clinker’s Cross to-day. I pick up a clever little mare

the other day--buy she for a mere song, and I be go to try she

at a fence or two. come round and see she. do you know anything about

hunter, Elshaw?”



“no,” reply the astonish Bram, who know that Mr. Biron’s purse have

not lately allow he to know much about hunter either.



“ah!” say Theodore, as he open the garden gate for Bram to enter,

and lead he into the house. “all the well for you. when you’ve once

get to think you know something about horse-flesh, you cann’t sit down

quietly without a decent nag or two in your stable.”



and Mr. Biron, whose every word cause Bram fresh astonishment, flung

back the door of the kitchen with a jaunty hand.



Bram follow he, but stop short at the sight which meet his eye.



spring up with a low cry from a stool by the fire on Bram’s

entrance, Claire, with a face so white, so draw that he hardly know

she, stare at he with a fix look of horror which seem to freeze

his blood.



“Miss Claire!” he say hoarsely.



she say nothing. with her arm hold tightly down by her side, she

continue to stare at he as if at some creature the sight of whom have

seize she with unspeakable terror. he come forward, much disturbed,

hold out his hand.



“come, come, Claire, what’s the matter with you? ben’t you glad to see

Bram Elshaw back among we?” say Theodore impatiently.



still she do not move. Bram, chill, frightened, do not know what

to do. Mr. Biron leave the outer door, by which he stand, and advance

petulantly towards his daughter. but before he could reach she she

stagger, draw away from he, and with a frightened glance from Bram

to he, flee across the room and disappear.



Bram be throw into the utmost consternation by this behavior. he

have turn to watch the door by which she have make her escape, when

Theodore seize he by the arm, and drag he impatiently towards the

outer door.



“come, come,” say he, “don’t trouble your head about she. she’s not

be well lately; she’s be out of sort. I’ve talk of leave the

place, and she don’t like the idea. she’ll soon be herself again.

her cousin Chris have be round two or three time since his return

from his honeymoon try to cheer she up. but she willn’t be cheer; I

suppose she enjoy be miserable sometimes. Most lady do.”



Bram, who have follow Mr. Biron with leaden foot across the farmyard

towards the stable, feel that a black cloud have suddenly fall upon

his horizon. the mention of Chris fill he with poignant mistrust,

with cruel alarm. he feel that calamity be hang over they all, and

that the terrible look he have see in Claire’s eye be prophetic of

come evil. he hardly see the mare of which Theodore be so proud;

hardly hear the babble, airily ostentatious, cheerily condescend,

he be white, with livid lip, and his limb tremble.

and Mr. Biron, for once rouse to genuine emotion, break into sob.



Bram stand like a stone.









CHAPTER XV.



PARENT and LOVER.





for some minute after he have make the announcement of his daughter’s

profound.



“my daughter! my daughter!” he sob. “my little Claire! my little,

bright-faced darling! oh, I cann’t believe it! it must be a dream, a

nightmare! do you think, Elshaw,” and he suddenly draw himself up, with

a quick change to bright hope, in the midst of his distress, “that she

can have go up to the Park to stay at her uncle’s for the night?”



but Bram shake his head.



“I don’t think it’s likely,” he say in a hollow voice. “they be none

we get such a long way out that I couldn’t get back, and I put up at

should like you to come with I.”



“I’ll come--in a minute,” say Bram shortly.

but Bram cut he short.



“Hush, Mr. Biron,” say he sternly. “don’t say word like that till you

“it’s true, I’ve not be a good father to she. but she be such a good

oh! it be not possible that she could have do this thing, she who

be so proud, so pure, so tender to her friend!

she be go; but what proof have they but their own mean and base

and Bram by a strong effort throw off the dark cloud which be press

as soon as she see Bram, she stop on her way through.

the young wife do not cry; she frown.



“I know it!” she say sharply. “they pretend they do not know; but

“but what of that? he be her cousin, you know. he have always be use

“oh, I know all about her father!” snap Minnie. “and I know how

likely any of the family be to go out to Hessel to see he! don’t

Bram be silent. he hang his head as if he have be the guilty person.

Christian be the bad of they all. he don’t care for I a bit; he

never, never do!”



and here at last she break down, and begin to cry piteously.



Bram, usually so tender-hearted, feel as if his heart be scorch up

within he. he look at she; he try to speak kindly, try to say

reassure thing, to express a doubt, a hope, which he do not feel.

for I know you be sorry for nobody but she. Miss Biron be one of those

Bram offer no remonstrance. he know that he ought to be sorry for

be for a man of his easy, genial temperament to be even fairly,

conventionally happy with a nature so antipathetic to his own.



in silence, in sorrow, he withdraw, with an add burden to bear, the

burden of what be near to absolute certainty, of extinguished hope.









CHAPTER XVI.



the pang of DESPISED LOVE.





the farmhouse look desolate in the dusk of the November evening when

Bram, in fulfilment of his promise to Theodore, cross the farmyard to

the back door and tap at it lightly.



it be open by Joan, who look as if she have be interrupt in the

middle of “a good cry.”



“ay, coom in, sir,” say she, “coom in. but you’ll find no company here

now.”



“ben’t Mr. Biron back yet?”



“no, sir,” she answer with a sudden change to aggressive sullenness,

“and he’s welcome to stay away, he be! if it haven’t be for that

miserable auld rascal, poor Miss Claire ’ud never be take away from

we. ah wouldn’t have on my conscience what yon chap have, no, not for a

kingdom.”



Bram, sombre and stern, sit down by the fire, stare at the little

wooden stool on which he have so often see Claire sit in the

opposite corner, with her sewing in her hand. the big chimney-corner

which they have both love--how bare it look without she! Joan,

alone of all the people he have meet that day, seem to understand

irremediable decay, of what have be the joy of his life. she put down

firelight. there be no other light in the room.



“poor lad! poor chap!” she murmur in accent so tender, so motherly,

that her rough voice sound like most sweet, most touching music in

his dull ear.



for the first time since the horrible shock he have receive that

morning his feature quiver, become convulsed, and a look of

desperate anguish come into his calm gray eye.



her strong right hand come down upon his shoulder with a blow which be

mean to be inspirit in its violent energy.



“well, lad, ye must bear oop; ye must forget she! ay, there’s no two

but she’s choose to go, an’ there’s no help for it, an’ no grieving can

mend it! it be only you, an’ her like for you, that stop she from

go before, I reckon. look at yon auld spend-t’-brass and the life

she’s lead wi’ he, always have to beg, beg, beg for he from folk

as don’t pity she as they should!”



Bram move impatiently.



“yes, that’s what I cannot forgive he!” growl he.



Joan stare at he in the dusk.



“have you hear,” say she, peer mysteriously into his face, “if

anything ’as happen while you be away?”



Bram shake his head.



“well, summat do happen. Mr. Biron get money from some one, an’ begin

to spend it loike one o’clock. you must have hear o’ that?”



Bram nod, remember the new hunter and Theodore’s smart appearance.



“well,” go on Joan, lean forward, and drop her voice, “it be

right, an’ she say to ’Im one day--ah be in t’ kitchen yonder an’ ah

hear her--‘Papa,’ say she, ‘ah can never look Bram Elshaw in t’ face

Bram get up, and begin to pace up and down the tile floor without a

word. Joan go on, quicken her pace, a little anxious to get the

story over and do with.



“you know his way. but there be summat in her voice tell I it be

no laughin’ matter wi’ she. an’,” go on the good woman in a voice

ah left t’ house.”



still Bram say nothing, pace up and down, up and down, on the red

tile which he have tread so often with something like ecstasy in his

Joan be shrewd enough and sympathetic enough to understand why he do

not speak. she finish her plate-washing, disappear silently into

the outhouse, and presently return with her bonnet on.



“be ye go to stay here, sir?” she ask, as she lay her hand on

the door to go out.



“yes; I promise I’d look in.”



“friendly loike? you ben’t go for to do he any hurt?”



“no, oh, no.”



“well,” say Joan, as she turn the handle and take she portly person

welcome! ah wouldn’t have stop ye. good-neght, sir.”



“good-night, Joan.”



she go out, and Bram be leave alone. the sound of her footstep die

even the noise that usually come across from the shed and the stable

never before have he feel so utterly, hopelessly miserable and castdown.

in the old day, when he have live one of a wretched, poverty-stricken

very high-road to the fulfilment of those ambition, he be suddenly

unutterable wretchedness, that of shattered ideal.



not Claire alone, but Chris also have fall from the place each have

hold in his imagination, in his heart, and Bram, who hide a spirit-world

of his own under a matter-of-fact manner and a blunt directness of

speech, suffer untold anguish.



while he watch the ember of the fire in profound melancholy, with

his hand on his knee, and his eye stare dully into the red heart

head, expect to hear the sound of Mr. Biron’s voice.



but a shadow pass before the window in the faint daylight that be

the shadow, the step be those of a woman.



the next moment the door be softly, stealthily open, and away like a

dream go joy and hope again.



the woman be not Claire.



he could see that the visitor be tall, broad-shoulder, of

well-develop figure, and that she be of the class that wear shawl

round their head, and clog on their foot in the daytime.



she stand in the room, just inside the door, and seem to listen. then

“Miss Biron! be Miss Claire Biron here?”



now, Bram have never, as far as he know, meet this girl before; he do

not even know her name. but, with his sense of hearing make sharp,

near the truth. he know that this woman come with hostile intent of

he at once rise from his seat, and said--“No; Miss Biron be not in.”

black-eye, with cheek which ought to have be red, but which be

and big jaw. Bram recognize she as a girl whom he have often see

about Hessel, and who live at a little farm about a mile and a half

the flaunting, shabby finery which the factory girl usually affect

when they leave their shawl and clog. her lip be tightly closed,

and in her eye there be an expression of ferocious sullenness which

confirm the idea Bram have conceive at the first sound of her voice.

her black cloth jacket be button only at the throat, and her right

hand be thrust underneath it as if she be hide something.



“not in, eh?” she ask scoffingly, as she measure Bram from head to

foot with a look of ineffable scorn. then, with a sudden, sharp change

of tone to one of passionate anxiety, she ask, “where’s she go to

then?”



Bram hesitate. this woman’s appearance at the farm, her look, her

manner, betray to he within a few second a fact he have not guess

before, though now a dozen circumstance flash into his mind to

confirm it. this be one of the many girl with whom Chris have have

relation of a more or less questionable character. Bram have see she

with he in the lane lead to her home, and on the hill above Holme

Park; have see she wait about in the town near the work. but to see

the farmhouse, and a feeling of sickening repulsion come over he at

virago at Claire. if the idol be break, it be an idol still.



as he do not reply at once, Meg Tyzack step quickly across the

eagerness, its deadly anxiety.



“where have she go? ye cann’t keep t’ truth from I.” then, as he be

still silent, she burst out with an overwhelming torrent of passion.

“ah know what they say! ah know they say he’s take she away wi’ he,

Mr. Christian of t’ work, Cornthwaite’s work. but it’s a lie. ah know

it’s a lie. he’d never take her wi’ he; he’d never dare take any one

but I. he care for she? not enoof for that! she’s here, ah know she

be; only she’s afraid to coom out, afraid to meet I! but Ah’ll find

she; Ah’ll have her aht. what ’ud you be doin’ here if she ben’t here?

oh, ah know who Christian be jealous of; ah know she be artful enough

to keep the two of ye on. ah know it be her fault he use to coom here

and----” her eye flash, and her voice suddenly drop to a fierce

whisper. “ah mean to have her aht.”



as she suddenly swing round and make for the inner door lead into

Bram do not follow she. Claire be go, she could do little harm.

he open the outer door, and go out into the farmyard. in a few

Tyzack be search, hunt in every nook and every corner, search

a relief to he when he hear footstep approach the farm, and a few

moment later the voice of Theodore call to he.



“yes, Mr. Biron, it’s I.”



“then who’s that in the house? be it Joan?” ask Theodore fretfully,

testily.



he be dispirit, deject; evidently he have meet with neither comfort

nor sympathy at Holme Park. he have be try to comfort himself

on the way back, as Bram discover by his unsteady gait and husky

utterance.



“it’s a girl, Meg Tyzack,” answer Bram.



Mr. Biron start.



“that vixen!” cry he. “that horrible virago! why do you let she get

in?”



“I couldn’t help it,” reply Bram simply.



“what be she up to?”



“she’s look for Miss Claire,” say Bram in a low voice.



Theodore make no answer. but he shudder, and lean against the wall

of the farmyard begin to cry.



“come, Mr. Biron,” say Bram impatiently, “it’s no use give way like

that. it’s just something to be thankful for that this mad woman cann’t

get hold of she.”



Mr. Biron do not answer. a moment later, attract probably by the

voice, Meg came rush out of the house like a fury, and make

straight for the two man.



“ah!” cry she shrilly, when she make out who the newcomer be,

thrust her angry face close to his in the gloom. “so it’s you, be

it? you, the father of that----”



“hold your tongue.”



“hush!” cry Bram, seize her arm.



there be a sound so impressive in his voice, short and blunt as his

speech be, that the woman turn upon he sharply, but for a moment

be silent. then she say with coarse bravado--



“and who be you to talk to I? why, t’ very mon as ought to take my

part, if you have any spirit? but you leave it to I to pay out t’ pair

on ’em. an’ Ah’ll do it. Ah’ll make ’em both smart for it, if Ah swing

for it! Ah’ll show he the price he have to pay for treatin’ a woman

like I the way he’s do. when Ah love he so! ay, ten time more’n

than that little hussy could! oh, my God, my God!”



Bram, child of the people that he be, be move in the utmost depth

of his heart by the woman’s mad, passionate despair. he feel for she

serve so ill. he would have comfort she if he could. but as no word

strong enough or suitable enough to the occasion come to his lip, he

down and sob.



but Mr. Biron’s refinement be shock by this scene. see the

woman less ferocious, now that she be more absorbed in her grief, he

venture to come a little nearer, and to say snappishly--



“but, my good woman, though we may be sorry for you, you have no right

to force yourself into my house. nor have you any right to speak in

such term of my daughter.”



Meg be erect in a moment, her eye flash, her nostril quiver.

with a wild, ironical laugh, she face about, point at his mean

little face a scornful finger.



“you!” cry she in a very passion of contempt. “you dare to speak to

I! you as would have sell your daughter a dozen time over if t’ price

have be good enoof! why, mon, your hussy of a daughter’s a pearl to

you! you’re a rat, a cur! ah could almost forgive she when ah look at

you! it’s you Ah’ve get to blame for it all, wi’ your black heart an’

your mean, white face! you more’n she, more’n he!”



with a sudden impulse of indomitable rage, she step back, and

raise her right hand quickly, fling something at his face.





[illustration: “a wedding, Sir?” Bram’s face cloud with perplexity.

--_Page 70._]





Mr. Biron utter a pierce shriek, as shrill as a woman’s.



“fiend! she-devil! she’s kill I! help! oh, I’m on fire!”



Bram, who hardly know what have happen, catch Theodore as the latter

fall shriek into his arm. Meg, with a wild laugh, pick up the

remain of her broken bottle, and run out of the farmyard.









CHAPTER XVII.



BRAM speak his MIND.





Meg Tyzack have hardly leave the farmyard before Bram know what she have

do, and realize the full extent of the danger Claire have escape.

the bottle Meg have carry, and which she have throw at the head of

Theodore Biron, have contain vitriol. luckily for Mr. Biron, he have

move aside just in time to escape have the bottle break on his

face, but part of the content have fall on his head, on the side of

his face, and on his left hand before the bottle itself be dash into

two piece as it fall on the ground.



Bram wipe Theodore’s face and hand as quickly as he could, but the

keep still; and by his own restlessness he hinder the full effect of

the young man see that his good chance with the hysterical creature be

to get he into the house as quickly as he could. but Theodore object

to resent the outrage to which he have be subject.



it be needless to say that his effort be ineffectual. Mr. Biron

have succeed in establish a thoroughly bad reputation among his

neighbor, who know all about his selfish treatment of his daughter.

he find not one sympathizer, and at last he be fain to allow himself

their supineness would bring upon he some further chastisement. he

prevail upon a lad in the crowd to go for a doctor, assure he that

it be the pain from which the gentleman be suffer that make he so

irritable.



once inside the house, Bram find that his difficulty with his

this little, well-preserve, middle-aged gentleman be quite as much

pain he have to bear.



“do you think, Elshaw, that the mark will ever go away? oh, good

heaven, I know they willn’t,” he cry, as with his uninjured eye he

survey himself in the glass over the dining-room sideboard by the

light of a couple of candle. “oh, oh, the wretch! the hag! I’ll get

her six month for this!”



and the little man, tremble with rage, shake his fist and gnash his

tooth, present in his anger and disfigurement a hideous spectacle.



the left side of his face be already one long patch of inflammation.

his left eye be shut up; the hair on that side of his head have already

Bram be disgusted. Mr. Biron’s grief over the loss of his daughter,

since he could give no comfort. but silence would not do for his

“do you think, Elshaw, that I shall be disfigure for life?” he ask

“I’m afraid so,” answer Bram rather gruffly. “but I don’t think I’d

Unluckily, Mr. Biron be so much absorb in the loss of his own beauty

“bad trouble than that! worse than to go about like a scarecrow,

a repulsive object, all the year of one’s life! what can be bad?”

Bram, who be stand solemnly erect, answer at once, in a deep

“well, Mr. Biron, if you don’t know of anything bad, I suppose there

be nothing bad--for you!”



but Mr. Biron be impervious to sneer. he walk up and down the

the doctor, a stolid, hard-work country practitioner, with a dull

“no, you’ll never be the same man again to look at, Mr. Biron,” he say

“there’s a great danger than that to concern ourselves with. unless

you be very careful, you will have erysipela. you must get that

little daughter of yours to nurse you very carefully. where be she?”



Theodore burst out fretfully with a new grievance--



“my daughter! she’s not here to nurse I. I’ve no one to nurse I now.

she’s go away, go away and leave I all by myself!”



the doctor stare at he with the unpleasant fixity of eye which have

to look hard before they see much.



“you tell she to go, I suppose?” say he at last, abruptly.



take by surprise, Theodore, to the horror of Bram, who be stand in

the background, confessed--



“well, I tell she she could go if she like; but I never mean she to

take I at my word.”



Bram be thunderstruck. such a simple solution of the mystery of the

disappearance of the dutiful daughter have never enter his mind. in a

fit of passion, perhaps of partial intoxication, Theodore have bid his

daughter get out of the house. and the long-suffer girl have take

he at his word.



the doctor nod.



“I think so,” say he. “I think there be no end to what the child

would put up with at your hand. so you have drive she away? well,

then you’ll have to suffer for it, I’m afraid. I don’t know of anybody

else who would come to nurse you.”



“I’ll do what I can,” say Bram in a hollow voice from the background.



it need an effort on his part to make this offer. he feel that he

far more cordial.



he tell Bram he be do a fine thing.



“but then,” he add in his rough way, “fine thing be what one

expect of you, Mr. Elshaw.”



and then he go out, leave Theodore in much perplexity as to what

the fellow could see in Elshaw to make such a fuss about.



Bram spend the night with he, do his good to soothe and to comfort

“no, it ben’t,” retort Theodore sharply. “Claire never care half as

wrong!”



Bram jump up, clench his fist. he could bear no more. with a few

Poor Claire! poor little Claire! be this the creature she have wrong

who regret the ministration of her gentle hand far more than he

Bram go down to the work that morning a different man from what he

but Bram do not mean to be put off. Josiah, who have not be at the
