Mr. Cornthwaite see he as soon as he himself arrive, and, evidently

with the intention of take the bull by the horn, speak to he at

once.



“ah, Elshaw, good-morning. come in here a moment, please. I want to

speak to you.”



Bram follow in silence, and stand within the room with his back to

the door, with a stern expression on his pale face.



Mr. Cornthwaite broach the unpleasant subject at once.



“nice business this, eh? nice thing Chris have do for himself now!

bring a hornet’s nest about his ear and mine too! Old Hibbs and his

wife have be down to my house blackguard I; Minnie herself be

fit for a lunatic asylum, and, to complete the business, the girl’s

rascally father have be to my house, try to levy blackmail. but

I’ve make up my mind to make short work of the thing! I start for

the scruff of the neck if necessary!”



“and--Claire--Miss Biron?” say Bram hoarsely.



“oh, she must shift for herself. she know what she be do, run

off with a married man. I’ve no pity for she; not the least. I wash my

hand of the pair of they, father and daughter, now. he must just pack

up his trap and be off after she. what become of she be his affair,

not ours!”



“Mr. Christian cann’t get rid of the responsibility like that, sir,”

say Bram, with a note of sombre warning in his voice.



“I take upon myself the responsibility for he,” retort Mr.

always have be. circumstance have confirm it most amply. a young

“I don’t think you will find yourself and Mr. Christian in agreement

“well, whether he agree or not, he’ll come back with I to-morrow,”

reply Mr. Cornthwaite hotly.



“then, Mr. Cornthwaite, you’ll please take my notice now, and I’ll

be out of this to-day. for,” Bram go on, with a rise spot of deep

“upon my word, yours be very singular morality for a young man who go

“do you think if you bring Mr. Christian back ‘by the scruff of the

she wouldn’t have come to none if you haven’t force on this curse

Mr. Cornthwaite’s black eye blaze. he hate even a semblance of

contradiction; and Bram’s determined and dog attitude irritate he

“your notion be charming in the abstract, Elshaw. I have no doubt,

too, that there be some section of society where your idea might be

move. if my son be to commit such an unheard-of folly as you suggest

you know enough of Christian to tell whether he would find life with

any young woman agreeable under those condition.”



Bram remain silent. there be a pause, rather a long one. then Mr.

Cornthwaite speak again----



“of course, you be sensible enough to understand that this be my

business, and my son’s; that it be a family matter, a difficulty

in which I have to act for the good. and I hope,” he go on in a

“as to that, sir, you have well let I go--and without notice,” say

bear in a different section of society, sir, I suppose. and so, as

Ah’ve love Mr. Christian, and as Ah’ve have much to thank you and he

for, sir, you’d well let I go back--to my own section of society,

where a man have to stand by his own deed, like a man!”



Mr. Cornthwaite’s attitude, his tone, change insensibly as he look

and listen to the man who tell he his view so honestly, and stand

by they so firmly. he see that Bram be in earnest, and he begin to

walk up and down the room, thinking, planning, consider. he do

not want to lose this clever young man; he could not afford to do so.

Bram have something like a genius for the detail of business, and be

besides as honest as the day; not a too common combination.



the young man wait, but at last, as Mr. Cornthwaite make no sign of

address he, he turn to touch the handle of the door. then Mr.

Cornthwaite suddenly stop in his walk, and make a sign to he to

stay.



“well, Elshaw,” say he in a more genial tone, “will you, if you must

go, promise I one thing? will you see Mr. Christian in my presence

first, and hear what he have to say for himself?”



Bram hesitate.



“I don’t want to hear anything,” say he sullenly. “I’d rather go, sir.”



“no doubt you would, but you wouldn’t like to treat we in any way

unfairly, would you, Bram? you acknowledge that we’ve not treat you

badly, you know.”



“yes, sir.”



“well, then, you can hardly refuse to hear what the culprit have to say

in his own defence. if, after hear he, you be not satisfied, you

can have the satisfaction of tell he what you think of he in good

round term before you go. now, be that a bargain? you stay here until

I come back from town--at least--with or without (for, of course, you

may be right, and he may not come) my son?”



Bram hesitate; but he could not well refuse.



“all right, sir. I’ll stay till you come back,” he answer sullenly.



and, without another word or another look, he accept his employer’s

satisfied motion of assent as a dismissal, and leave the room.









CHAPTER XVIII.



face to FACE.





doggedly, sullenly, with a hard mouth and cold eye, Bram go about

his day’s work in the office. his fellow-clerk know that something of

deep import have happen during that half-hour while he be shut up

with Mr. Cornthwaite in the inner room; but so well do they know he

by this time that no one make any attempt to learn from he what it be

that have pass.



quietly, unostentatiously, without any apparent effort, Bram have

make himself a unique position, with his office companion as well

as with his employer. very taciturn, very stolid of manner, never

give an unasked opinion on any subject, he always seem to be too

much absorb in the detail of work to have time or inclination for

the discussion, the idle chatter, with which the rest beguile the

monotonous hour on every opportunity.



but they have long since cease to “chaff” he on his attitude, not

through any distaste on his part for this form of attack, but as a

natural result of the respect he inspire, and of the position he hold

with “the guv’nor” and his son. there be a feeling that he would be

“boss” himself some day, and a consequent disposition to leave he

alone.



but when the day’s work be do, and Bram start on the walk back to

Hessel, the look of dogged attention which his face have wear during

office hour relax into one of keen anxiety. he have be able, by

force of will, to thrust into the background of his mind the one

subject which be all-important to he. now that he be again, for

fifteen hour, a free man, his thought fasten once more on Claire

and on the question--would Christian, obedient to his father and to

self-interest, abandon she, or would he not?



Bram feel a dread of the answer. he would not allow to himself that

he believe Christian capable of what he look upon as an act of

inconceivable baseness; but down at the bottom of his heart there be a

dumb misgiving, an unacknowledged fear.



and Bram, his thought stretch out beyond the limit he impose

upon they, ask himself what he should do for the good for the poor

child, if she be leave strand, as Mr. Cornthwaite make no secret of

intend. he have unconsciously assume to himself, now that the image

of Claire have be depose from the high pedestal of his ideal, the

attitude of guardian to this most helpless of creature, take upon

himself in advance the position which her father ought to have hold.



if she be abandon by her lover, it be he who would find she out,

and care for she, and settle she in some place of safety. that she

would never come back to the neighborhood of her own accord Bram feel

sure.



when Bram get back to Hessel, he call at once at the farm, with a

linger hope that something might have be hear of Claire, that she

might have send some message, write some letter to her father or to

Joan.



but she have not. he find Mr. Biron in the care of Joan, whose patience

he try severely by his fretfulness and irritability. the doctor have

call again, and have express a grow fear of erysipela, which have

only increase the patient’s ill-temper, without make he any more

and Joan report that he have be do so all day, and that there be

no reasoning with he or stop he, even by use the authority of

the doctor.



Theodore be by this time in a maudlin and tearful condition, bewail

now the flight of his daughter, and now his own wound, without cease.



Bram do what he could to cheer he, and to persuade he to a more

reasonable course of conduct, but the effect be hardly more than

momentary. and on the following day his condition have undoubtedly

become bad. Bram, however, be oblige to leave he to go to the

office, where the day pass without incident. Mr. Cornthwaite have go

up to town on the previous night, and have not return. Bram begin to

hope that Christian have refuse to come back.



two more day pass, during which Mr. Biron’s symptom grow bad.

the erysipela have not only declare itself on the wounded part of the

he do about the revenge he want for his own injury, Joan be so

bluntly outspoken on the subject that even he have to give up the idea.



“you’d good tak’ it quiet, sir,” say the good woman coolly. “you see

you couldn’t coom into Coort wi’ clean hand yourself, wi’ the Joodge

and everybody knowin’ the life as Miss Claire lead with you. Happen ye’d

get tell it serve you roight!”



and Bram concurring, though less outspokenly, the indignant Theodore

CHAPTER XIX.



SANCTUARY.

CHAPTER XX.



by the FURNACE fires.

t’ hill!’ and she mean it, she do! ah doan’t like t’ look of she.

Bram be silent for some minute.



“well, it cann’t go on like this,” say he at last. “the creditor will

and Chris, give he a familiar, affectionate push towards the door of

through the heart of the work, which stretch for hundred of acre

it be Christian Cornthwaite.









CHAPTER XXI.



the FIRE go OUT.

fortunately, help be at hand. a man, one of the hand employ at the

such first aid as it be possible to give Bram be already give. but

then Bram call to she.



“come. he want you to come.”

“Bram!”

CHAPTER XXII.



CLAIRE’S CONFESSION.





Bram hold the hand of his dead friend for some minute, not dare to

but Claire have disappear.

Bram be awe by the depth of his so suddenly manifest despair. he

he have not to go far. he have not, indeed, walk twenty pace, when,

that of Bram, and stop short in the middle of the floor at the same

“he’s go oop to t’ park,” say Joan, with a look at Bram as much as

to say there be no help for it, and the truth must come out.



Claire, sink on the near chair, utter a short, hollow laugh.

desperately tired, and, on the other hand, she be anxious to get back

admit again.

home for an hour, would you stay here till ah coom back? Ah’d give a

look in to see all be reght. she doan’t look as if she ought to spend

t’ neght by herself.”



this be say in a low voice to Bram, whom she have beckon to the door

of the back kitchen, while Claire remain in the same attitude of

deep depression at the table.



“no,” say he at once. “she mustn’t be leave alone to-night. I’ll stay

till you come back, whether her father come back before then or not.

she’s have a great shock--an awful shock. but,” and he glance back at

the motionless girl, “I willn’t tell you about it now. and you can go

now. you needn’t trouble about the tea; I’ll make it.”



Joan look at he, and then at Claire with round, apprehensive eye.



“will she let ye stay?” she ask, in a dubious whisper.



“poor child, yes. she’s almost forget who I be.”



but Claire have lift up her head, and be rise to come towards they.

Claire follow Joan with dull eye as the good woman, with a series of

boil. she do not interrupt he, and when he glance at she again he

heavily upon her hand, lean on the table drowsily.



presently she make a little moan noise, and begin to move her head

“got a headache, Miss Claire?”



she raise her head as if it be a weight too heavy for she to lift

“oh, Bram, it’s so bad, bad than I’ve ever have before,” say she

in her eye there be no long any grief; only a dull sense of great

he see that she have no strength leave to do anything for herself. she

“no. don’t make I go in there. let I stay here.”



there be an old break-down horsehair cover sofa against the wall

a strange vigil this! by the side of the woman who have be so much

and Bram hardly blame he for it. he would not have have he feel the

as this thought pass through his mind, and instantly become a

“Bram,” she say in her old voice, bright and soft and clear, “I

“it seem a strange thing to have forget. but be it Tuesday?”



Bram nod slowly, as if the head he bend have be weight with lead.

Bram pat her hot, twitch hand reassuringly.



“he be quite safe, I’ve no doubt,” he say. “he’s go out, and he

haven’t come back yet.”



Claire stare at he inquiringly, and frown as if in perplexity.



“but what have happen?” she ask. “why do everything seem so

be save the trouble of find answer to any of her question except

“Joan will be back in a little while,” say he. “she’s go home to see

to her child. but she willn’t be long.”



“be she come back to-night? why be she come back to-night?”



“well, to look after you.”



“then I have be ill?”



“you’re not very well now,” say Bram gently.



“why not? something have happen? willn’t you tell I what it be?”



there be a pause. then she give his hand an affectionate, cling

pressure.



“never mind, Bram. you needn’t tell I unless you like. I don’t mind

anything when you’re here. you willn’t go away, will you?”



the love tone, the caress manner, stir his heart to the

depth. surely this tender trust be her own real feeling for he,

coldness. what do it mean? be this the woman who have ruin she

“Bram, what be the difficulty? there be a difficulty, ben’t there?

only I cann’t remember what it be. why be it that you stay away?

that you don’t come here as you use to? you don’t know what a long

time it seem, and how I use to long for you to come back again!

why, I use to watch for you when I know it be time for you to go

“oh, my darling!” burst from Bram’s lip in spite of himself.



that one word be answer enough for she. she smile happily up into his

as Bram sit, as still as a statue lest he should disturb she by a

rain, begrime with mud, a horrible spectacle of fear and terror, stole

into the room, and shut the door, bolt it, and then sink in a

heap on the floor, with his eye turn in a ghastly panic of alarm

towards the window.









CHAPTER XXIII.



FATHER and DAUGHTER.





Bram be strike by the entire change which have take place in Theodore

Biron, a change which have, indeed, be creep over he ever since

Meg’s attack, and his consequent disfigurement, but which seem to

have culminate to-night in what be almost a transformation.



as he crouch on the floor, and look anxiously up at the window,

there be no trace in the cower, shrivelled figure, in the scarred,

inflame face, out of which the bloodshot eye peer in terror, of the

gay, easy-mannered country gentleman _en amateur_, who have impress

Bram so strongly with his airy lightness of heart only sixteen month

before.



“lock the door, Bram,” say he, presently, in a hoarse voice when he

suddenly become conscious of the young man’s presence. “lock the door!”



Bram hasten to do so. he want to open it first to look out and see

who it be that have inspire Mr. Biron with so much alarm. but Theodore

restrain he by a violent gesture.



“lock it, lock it!” repeat he, as, evidently relieve to find a

man in the house, he get up from the floor, and go with shiver

limb and chatter tooth towards the fire. “and now bolt the

shutter--quick--and then on the other side!”



he indicate with a nod the front of the house, but when Bram walk

towards the door he shuffle after he, as if afraid of be leave

alone. Bram turn to cast a glance at the sofa and its occupant before

leave the room. Theodore, in a state of nervous alarm which make he

watch every look, glance back also. on see his daughter lie back

with closed eye on the cushion, he utter a cry.



“Claire, oh, oh, what will become of she? what will become of I?”



and, utterly break down, he cover his face with his shiver hand,

and sob loudly.



Bram wonder if he have hear all.



“come, come, be a man, Mr. Biron,” say he. “what be it you’re afraid

of?”



“that sh--she--devil who--who half-blind I, who throw that stuff

over I!” sob Theodore. “she’s follow I--from Holme Park--I

manage to dodge she among the tree of the park; but she know where I

live. she’ll come here, I know she will.” suddenly he draw himself up,

in another spasm of fear. “see that the door be lock in the front,

and the window--see to they!” cry he, with a burst of energy.



“all right,” say Bram. “I’ll see to that. you stay here with she,” and

he indicate Claire with a movement of the head.



but Mr. Biron shrink into himself, and try to follow Bram out.



“I’m afraid of she! she’s go mad; I know she have,” whisper he.

“haven’t you hear what she do to-night--down at the work?”



and Theodore, whose face have in a moment go ashy white, all but the

inflame patch on the left side, which have become a livid blue, creep

close still to Bram. but the young man’s face as he again look

“you’re right, Mr. Biron. the poor child be mad, I believe,” he say

gravely. “and, thank God, she haven’t come to herself yet. one could

almost wish,” he add, more to himself than to his companion, “that

she never may.”



Mr. Biron shudder.



“do you mean that she be ill?” he ask querulously.



“yes, she’s very ill--delirious.”



Mr. Biron shoot right out of the room into the hall with all his old

agility. he be evidently as much afraid of his unhappy daughter as he

“oh, these woman, these woman! they never can keep their head!” moan

Bram, who have leave the door of the kitchen open so that he might be

he be too much use to Theodore’s utter selfishness to feel more than

he detain Bram for a few moment, however, to tell he of his

adventure at Holme Park.

you may hardly believe it, but they don’t want even to let I do that.

but I insist; a desperate man will do much, and I make such a noise

will understand that there be anything in the world to be respect

except money! and, after all, can one blame they when their master

and mistress be no well? it’s all money, money, with Josiah

Cornthwaite!”



Bram, who be anxious to get back to the kitchen that he might keep

watch over Claire, cut he short.



“well, and Mr. Cornthwaite? he arrive at last?”



Theodore’s face fall at the remembrance.



“Ye-es, and I shall never forget what he do, what he say. he come

what do you think of that? what do you think of that? and I, in my weak

state, to hear it! I give you my word, Elshaw, I never think I should

get home alive!”



there be a pause. Mr. Biron wipe his face. his hand be shake;

his voice be tremulous and hoarse. he look as pitiful a wretch as it

be possible to imagine.



“do he tell you--how it happen?” ask Bram in a low voice.



he be hope, always hope against hope, that some new fact would

cousin----”

from Bram’s lip.



Theodore grow suddenly quiet, and stare at he apprehensively.



“how be she force to do it?” he ask querulously.



but Bram do not answer.



“well, yes. what else do Mr. Cornthwaite say?” ask he.



“and that they quarrel close to the railway line. and that

she--she--’pon my soul, I cann’t see how it’s possible--a little bit of

Bram be frown, deep in thought. again he do not make any answer.

Elshaw?”



“I be there,” reply Bram simply.



this give Mr. Biron a great shock. he begin to shiver again, and

“well,” say he in a complain voice, as he begin to drag himself up,

Bram feel rather remorseful when he see how the little man take his

“who’s that?” she say in a husky voice of alarm.



Bram, who have hear nothing, listen too. and then he find that her

ear be keener than his own, for in another moment there come Joan’s

he let she in, and see at once that she have hear something of the

she look at Claire with eloquent eye.

“that she do it, that our poor, little Miss Claire kill he, kill

shocking accident, if she do; of that you may be sure.”



“but will they be able to prove that?” ask the good woman anxiously.



“we’ll hope they may,” say he gravely. “in the meantime she’s so ill

Joan set about this task with only the delay cause by the necessity

touch he by its pathetic lack of meaning.

“no, don’t take I away,” she say. “I want to stay here to talk to

Bram.”



and she stretch out feebly over Joan’s shoulder two little hand

towards he.

and then, with a sudden return to the extreme docility she have show to

he all the evening, she smile, and let her hand and her head fall as

Joan start with her burden on the way upstairs.









CHAPTER XXIV.



MR. BIRON’S REPENTANCE.





then Bram go upstairs also, and knock at Mr. Biron’s door.



“I’m go for the doctor now, Mr. Biron,” he call out without

enter. “I’ve come up to ask if there’s anything I can get for you

before I go.”



“come in, Elshaw, come in!” cry Theodore, in a voice full of

tremulous eagerness. “I want to speak to you.”



Bram obey the summon, and find himself for the first time in Mr.

Biron’s bedroom, which be the most luxurious room in the house. a

bright fire burn in the grate, this be a luxury Theodore always

indulge in during the winter; the bed and the window be hang with

handsome tapestry, and there be book-shelf, table, arm-chair,

he himself be sit, wrap in a cozy dressing-gown, with his foot

that it be give he uneasiness.

he utter the word, as be natural in such a man, as if the whole

pursue the conscience-stricken man. “I know I do wrong in let she

“you send she? begging, of course, as usual?”



the word be harsh enough, brutal, perhaps, in the circumstance.

Bram be silent. these word chime in so well with the hope he would

“wait,” he say petulantly. “didn’t I say I have something to tell you?

it’s something that concern Claire, too.”

“you remember that a box be send to you--a chest, by the man at East

Grindley who leave you his money?”

self-accusation.



“I go to your lodge the day it come, just to see that it be safe.

your landlady have send to ask I if I could take care of it for you, as

it be something of value. but I prefer to leave the responsibility

with she. in--in fact, Claire think it well too.”



Bram read between the line here, know what strong reason poor

“there be a key send with it.”



Bram look up. he have find no key, and have be oblige to force the

padlock.



“the key be in a piece of paper. I find it on the mantelpiece.

I--I--well, of course, I have no right to do it; but I think it would

be well for I to look over the content of the chest to make sure

they be not tamper with in your absence.”



Bram be attentive enough now.



“so I unlock the box, and I just glance through the thing it

contain. you know what I find; with the exception of this, that

there be some loose cash----”



Bram’s face grow red with sudden perception. but he make no remark.



“I forget exactly what it be, something between two and three hundred

they to our press necessity. I argue with myself that you would

and--in short, I may have be wrong, but I--borrow they.”



a strange light have break on Bram’s face.



“do Miss Claire know?” he ask suddenly in a ringing voice.



“well--er--yes, in point of fact she do. she come to look for I, and

Bram’s brain be burst. his heart be beat fast. he come quickly

Mr. Biron be disconcert.



“well, er--I think that--that perhaps, until I could see my way to

but Bram do not wait for more explanation. indeed, he need no

Mr. Biron be quick to take advantage of this disposition in Bram. his

this thrust, cause by a sudden remembrance of the hunter and the new

Josiah stop short in his walk when the two man enter. he nod to

and go upstairs, follow by Joan.



“will you come through, sir?” say Bram. “there’s a fire in the

“no, sir. she follow he, but he lose sight of she before he get

“you bring she back home, I suppose?”

“and how do you propose to do that?”



“Sir, you know well.”



his voice shake, and he stop. there be silence between they till

Mr. Cornthwaite open the door.



“well, Doctor,” say he, “what of the patient?”



there be more impatience than solicitude in his tone.



“they’re both very ill,” answer the doctor. “they ought each to have

“very well. can you engage they, Doctor? I’ll undertake to pay all the

the doctor be impress by this generosity; so be Bram, but in a

“what’s the matter with they?” go on Mr. Cornthwaite in the same

“Pneumonia in Mr. Biron’s case, bring on by exposure to wet and cold,

“and the daughter?”



“acute congestion of the brain. she’s delirious.”



“ah!”



Mr. Cornthwaite seem satisfied now that he have the doctor’s assurance

that the illness be genuine. he make no more inquiry, but he

doctor perceive that it be lock and bolt at the top and bottom.



“all right,” say he, “I’ll go through the other way.”



and he make his way to the kitchen, follow by Mr. Cornthwaite and

Bram.



as he open the door which lead into the kitchen, the wind blow

be sweep in, and the tablecloth be blow off into his face as he

prepare something the doctor have order, make her appearance at the

“I shouldn’t leave this door open,” say the doctor as he cross the

room to shut it. “the wind blow through the whole house.”



Joan stare.



“ah don’t leave it open, sir,” say she. “Ah’ve only just coom through

here, and it be shut then. some one’s be and open it.”



Bram give a glance round the room, and then open the door through

which he and the other have just come to examine the hall.



“what’s the matter?” ask Mr. Cornthwaite sharply. he have bid the

Bram, with a candle in his hand, be peer into the dark corner.



“I be just think, sir, that perhaps Meg Tyzack have get in while we

be talk in the drawing-room,” say he. “Mr. Biron make I bolt the

door to keep she from get in. he seem to be afraid she would

follow he into the house.”



the word be hardly utter, when from the floor above there come a

pierce scream, a woman’s scream.



“Claire!” shout Bram, spring on the stair.



but before he could mount half a dozen step a wild figure come out of

Claire’s room, and rush to the head of the staircase in answer to his

call. but it be not Claire. it be, as Bram have fear, Meg Tyzack,

recognizable only by her deep voice, by her loud, hoarse laugh, for the

figure itself look scarcely human.



stand at the top of the stair, with her arm outstretche as if to

prevent any one’s pass she on the way up, the gaunt creature seem

witch in the throe of prophecy.



“stand back! stand back! leave she alone!” she cry furiously, as Bram

“what have she do? what have she do?” ask Mr. Cornthwaite in a

“what have she do? what have I do?” say she in a horrible whisper.

“why, I’ve do the good night’s work that’s ever be do on this

earth, that’s what I’ve do. I’ve send the man and the woman I hate

both to----. ha! ha! ha!”



with a shrieking laugh she leapt past he to the bottom of the stair.









CHAPTER XXV.



MEG.





Bram Elshaw hear Meg’s wild word as he rush along the corridor

towards the room out of which she have just come--Claire’s room, as he

guess, with a sob of terror rise in his throat.



the door be open. on the floor, just inside, lie what Bram at first

think to be Claire’s lifeless body. Meg have drag she off the bed,

and fling she down in an ecstasy of mad rage.



but even as he raise she in his arm, before the frightened Joan have

she be still breathe. Joan want to send he away.



“leave she to I, sir, leave she to I. you can goa and fetch t’ doctor

but Bram do not seem to hear she. he be stare into the unconscious

face, and shine in his gray eye.



“oh, Claire, Claire, my little Claire, my darling, be you go away?

be you go to die?”



the word break from his lip, hoarse, low, force up from his heart.

he do not know that he have utter they; do not know that he be not

“put she down; do ee put she down,” she say peremptorily. “do ye go

for to think as your call to she will do she any good? Goa ee for t’

doctor. and God forgive I for speak harsh to ye, sir.”





[illustration: “oh Claire, Claire, my little Claire, be you go to

die?”--_Page 200._]





and the good woman, see the strange alteration which come over

Bram lift Claire from the floor with the easy strength of which his

then he hold one of her hand for a moment, lean over she, and kiss

“a’ reght,” mutter he to Joan, fall once more into the broad

Yorkshire he have drop for so long, “Ah’m go.”



at the foot of the stair he be bring suddenly to full remembrance

“where be you go?” ask he shortly.



“for the doctor again, sir. Meg have nearly do for she, for Miss

Mr. Cornthwaite utter a short exclamation, which might have be

“and the old man--Mr. Biron, what do she do to he?”



Bram be silent. he remember Meg’s ferocious word, her triumphant

of Theodore’s room. but Mr. Biron be lie quietly in bed, apparently

“Mr. Biron’s all right, sir,” he say to Mr. Cornthwaite, who have by

it be very late when a nurse, the only one to be get on the spur of

“what, you here still, Mr. Elshaw?” cry she.

“why, sir, ye’d good goa whoam,” say Joan kindly. “ye can do no good,

“ah, but I’ve something to say to you, Joan. look here; don’t it seem

Joan interrupt he with a profound nod.



“Lawk-a-murcy, ay, sir. Ah’ve thowt o’ that too,” say she in an eager

Bram spring up.



“Joan, that’s what I’ve be think myself ever since the woman

Christian must have be the man!”



they stare each into the face of the other, full of strong excitement,

“you’ve get it, Mr. Bram, you’ve get it, ah believe!” cry she in a

be as innocent of it as t’ new-bear babe!”



full of this idea, Bram conceive the thought of make inquiry at

Meg’s own home, and he start at once with this object.



it be now very late, past eleven o’clock; but his uneasiness be

and arrive at his destination within an hour.



the rain have cease by this time, and the moon peep out from time

to time, and from behind a mass of straggle cloud. the little farm

lie in a nook between two hill, and as Bram draw near he see that a

light be still burn within. in get over a gate he make a little

“Meg, be that you, Meg?” ask a woman’s voice anxiously.



“no,” say Bram, “it ben’t Meg, ma’am. it’s I, from Hessel, come to

ask if she’d get safe home.”



she come nearer, and peer into his face.



“and who be you?”



“my name’s Bram Elshaw. I’m a friend of the Birons at Duke’s farm.”



“ah!”



there be a world of sorrow, of significance, in the exclamation. after

“then maybe you know all about it? maybe you can tell I more than I

know myself? have you see anything of Meg--she’s my daughter--this

Bram hesitate. the woman go on--



“oh, don’t be afraid to speak out, sir, if it’s bad news. we’ve be

use to that of late; ever since our girl take up with t’ gentleman

mind, I do believe, sir. she wander about, so I often have to sit up

half t’ night for she, and she never give I a hand now with t’ farm

work. and as neat a hand in t’ dairy as she use to be! well, sir, what

be it? have she make away with herself?”



“she come to Duke’s Farm to-night, and attack Miss Biron,” say Bram.



“well, she be jealous,” say Meg’s mother, who seem to be less

afflict with sentiment concern her daughter than with vexation at

too late to stop they!”



Bram remember the night on which he have hear the voice in the

dismantle cottage, and he remember also with shame that he have

conceive the idea that Christian’s companion might be his cousin.



“do she tell you where she be go when she go out to-night?”

“she haven’t be home since this afternoon,” reply Meg’s mother. “she

go out before tea, mutter in her usual way threat against he

use to her raving; I don’t think she’d do any real harm unless to

herself, poor lass!”



“I’m afraid she have this time,” say Bram gravely. “I don’t know

anything more than I’ve tell you; but I’m afraid you must be prepare

startle, the woman press for an explanation. Bram, have really

but his suspicion be so strong that he feel no diffidence about

prepare Meg’s mother for a dreadful shock. on the other hand, he be

disorder state of mind would be consider in the view take of her

then he return to Hessel, try the door of Duke’s farm, and find it

lock for the night. he go round to the front, look up at the dim

then climb the hill to his own lodging.



on inquiry at the farm next morning on his way to his work Bram learn

from the nurse, who be the only person he could see, that while

he go on his way feel a little more hopeful, after impress upon

on arrive at the work, he see, as be to be expect after the

tragedy of the precede evening, an unusual stir among the workman,

who be stand about the entrance, talk in eager and excited

t’ fresh news.”



“what’s that?” ask Bram.



“coom this weay, sir; Ah’ll show ye.”



Bram, with a sick terror at his heart, ask himself what new horror

an exclamation break from Bram’s lip.



“not another--accident--here?”



for there be quite a large throng of workman scatter over the line

“ay, sir, it’s a woman this time.” and his voice suddenly fall to a

craze, for sure! she get in here, nobody know how, this morning; an’

she must ha’ throw herself down on t’ line pretty nigh t’ place where

train. anyway, we fahnd her lyin’ there this mornin’, poor lass!”



Bram have reach the group. he force his way through, and look down

roof of which Chris have die.



the mutilated body, which have be decapitate by the heavy wheel of

that of Meg Tyzack.



Bram stagger away, with his hand over his eye.









CHAPTER XXVI.



the GOAL reach.





no soon have Bram recover himself, and go to the office without

another question to any one, avoid the group and the sicken sight

they surround, than he find one of the servant from Holme Park with

a letter from Mr. Cornthwaite, ask he to come up to the house at

once.



he find his employer sit in the study alone, in the very seat,

the very attitude, he have see he in so often. while outside the

house look mournful in the extreme with its draw blind; while the

servant move about with silent step and scared face, the master sit,

apparently as unchanged as a rock after a storm.



it be not until a change of position on the part of Mr. Cornthwaite

deepen, the white patch in his hair grow wider, that the young

he have summon Bram to talk about business. and this he do with as

clear a head, as deep an apparent interest as ever. even the necessary

reference to his lost son he make with scarcely a break in his voice.



“I shall only have the work shut on one day, the day of the funeral,

Elshaw,” say he. “but in the meantime I shalln’t be down there myself.

I--I----” at last his voice falter. “I should like to be at work

again myself--to give I something to think about, instead of think

always on the same unhappy subject. but I couldn’t go down there so

soon after--after what I see there.”



Bram could not answer. the remembrance be too fresh in his own mind.



“so I want you to take my place as far as you can. you can telephone

through to I if you want to know anything. you have to fill your own

place now, you know Elshaw, and--another’s.”



Bram bow his head, deeply touch.



“now you can go. if you want to see--he, one of the servant will take

you up. and the lady, poor thing, be sure to be about. they bear up

beautifully, beautifully. his wife bear up a little too well for my

taste. but--perhaps--we must forgive she!”



he shake Bram by the hand, and the young man go out.



in the death-chamber upstairs he find Mrs. Christian, dry-eye,

on her knee beside the bed. she spring up on Bram’s entrance, and

remain beside he, without speak a word, while he look long

and earnestly at the placid face, look handsomer in death than it

have ever look in life, the waxen mask, refined and delicate beyond

expression, the long golden moustache, the fair hair, silky, smooth

than Bram have ever see they.



and presently a mist come before his eye, and he go hastily out.



he find Mrs. Christian still beside he. she be very pale, but quite

calm.



“I be glad you be come. you be poor Christian’s great friend, be

you not?” say she.



“yes, madam,” say Bram rather stiffly.



her little chirping voice irritate he. although he understand that

the neglect, unloved wife could not be expect to feel Christian’s

death as those do who have love and be love by he, he wish she

would not bear up quite so well, just as Mr. Cornthwaite have do.



but she insist on follow he downstairs, and then she open the

door of the morning-room, and ask he to come in. she would take no

excuse; she would not keep he a moment.

she have hear of it already then! Bram be appal by the manner in

“and, of course,” she go on, “I cannot feel that I have any right

Bram be overwhelm with relief unspeakable. this be the first time

he feel, and to put this question--



“do Mr. Cornthwaite--do his father--do Mr. Cornthwaite know that he

have do his son and Miss Biron--an injustice, think what he do?”



“why, of course he know,” reply Mrs. Christian promptly. “when he

find Christian in London he accuse he at once, and, of course,

Christian tell he--indeed, he could see for himself--he be wrong.

Christian know no more where his cousin have go to than anybody else

do.”



Bram be silent. he resent Mr. Cornthwaite’s behavior in leave he

in ignorance of such a fact. but his resentment be swallow up in

ineffable joy.



“what I want to learn be whether Miss Biron have all the nursing she

want,” chirp in little Mrs. Christian, “because I should be quite

glad to do anything I could for she out of christian charity. I have

do a good deal of sick nursing, and I like it,” pursue the poor,

little woman. “and I should be really glad of something to occupy my

thought now in this dreadful time. I have be live with my parent,

you know, since this misunderstanding first come about. his father

bring Christian here, and when he get well he show no wish to come

back. but when I hear late last night of what have happen, of course

I come here at once. and you will ask Miss Biron if she will have I,

willn’t you? I would nurse she well. and, indeed, they be not very kind

to I here.”



over the round, pale, freckle face there pass a quiver of feeling

which awake Bram’s sympathy at last. the unattractive little woman

have be rather cruelly treat from first to last in this affair of

Christian’s marriage. the Cornthwaites, one and all, have think much

of he and little of she from the beginning to the end of the matter.

and the offer to tend the girl Christian have love so much well than

herself have in it something touching, even noble, in Bram’s eye.



he stammer out that he would ask; that she be very good; that he

thank she heartily. then, exchange with she a hand-pressure which

be warm on both side, he leave she, and go out of the gloomy house.



of course, Joan would not hear of accept the kindly-offer service

of poor Mrs. Christian. but when she hear of the welcome information

which Bram have obtain from she she go half-mad with a delight which

find expression in clumsy leap and twirl and hand-clapping, and

even tear.



“and so it’s all reght, all reght, as we might ha’ know from t’

first. oh, we ought to die o’ shame to think as we ever thowt anything

different! oh, sir, an’ now ye can marry her reght off, an’ we can all

be happy as long as we live! oh, sir, this be a happy day!”



Bram try to silence she, try at least to check this confident

expression of her hope for the future. not that his own heart do not

beat high: if she be happy in this newly-acquire knowledge, he be

happy still. the idol be restore to its pedestal. it be he now,

and not she, who have a shameful secret--the secret of his past doubt

of she.



Bram could not forgive himself for these, could not now conceive that

they have be natural, justifiable. he have doubt she, the pure of

creature, as she be the noble, the sweetest. he feel almost that he

have sin beyond forgiveness, that he should never dare to meet she

frank eye again.



in the meantime, as day after day pass slowly by, the news he get of

she grow well, while that he receive of her father grow bad.



at last, two day after the funeral of Christian, he learn, when he

make his usual morning inquiry at the farm on his way down to the

work, that Mr. Biron have pass away quietly during the night.



his last word, utter at half-past two in the morning, have be a

characteristic request that somebody would go up immediately to Holme

Park with a note to Mr. Cornthwaite.



Bram hear from Joan that they try to keep the intelligence of her

father’s death from Claire, who be now much well, but who be still

by the doctor’s order keep very quiet. but she guess something from

the look and sound she hear, and before the day be over she have

learn the fact they try to conceal; and then she spend the rest of

the day in tear.



Mrs. Cornthwaite and Hester visit she on the follow day, and

beg she to come back with they. but Claire refuse very courteously,

but without be quite able to hide she feeling that their offer of

kindness and of sympathy come too late.



as, however, the farm and everything Mr. Biron have leave be to be

sell, it be necessary that she should go somewhere. so, on the

day after the funeral, Claire return to the cottage of the old

housekeeper at Chelmsley, who have write invite she most warmly to

return.



Bram, who have not dare to ask to see she, feel more diffidence in

approach she than he have ever do before, feel a pang whenever he

pass the desolate farmhouse on his way to and from his work. all the

news he get of Claire be through Joan, who receive from the grateful

and affectionate girl letter which she could not answer without great

difficulty and many appeal to her child, who have have the advantage

of the School Board.



Joan gradually become sceptical as the time go on as to the

fulfilment of her old wish that Bram should marry Claire. winter

melt into spring, and yet he make no effort to see she; he send she

no message, and she, on her side, say very little about he in her

letter. indeed, as the leave begin to peep out on the tree, there

crop up occasional reference in those same letter of her to the

kindness of a curate, who be teach she to sketch, and encourage

she to take such simple pleasure as come in her way.



Joan spelt out one of the letter which refer to these occupation

to Bram on the next occasion of their meeting. then she look up with

a broad smile, and give he a huge nod.



“Ye’ll get leave in the lurch, Mr. Elshaw, that’ll be t’ end of it!” she

say, with great emphasis.



“well,” say Bram with apparent composure, “if she take he, it will

be because she like he. and if she like he, why shouldn’t she have

he?”



but he be ill-pleased for all that. the vague hope he have long ago

cherish have become strong, more definite of late; he have force

himself to be patient, to wait, tell himself that it would be

indelicate to intrude upon the grief, the horror of the awful shock

from which she must still be suffer.



he have long since hear all the particular of the terrible death of

Chris, and of the manner in which the mistake between Meg and Claire

have come to be make. a workman have see Christian and Claire in earnest

conversation not far from the railway line; have see she give he the

note from her father which have bring she down. Christian have speak

kindly to she, have bend over she as if with the intention of kiss

she, when suddenly the stalwart figure of Meg, who have follow they

from some corner where she have conceal herself in the work, rush

between they, threaten they both with wild word. Claire have creep

away in alarm, and Meg have gradually drag Chris, talk, volubly

gesticulate all the time, out upon the railway line. she must have

calculate to a nicety the hour at which the next train might be

expect, so the general opinion afterwards run. at any rate, it be

she who be with Christian when the train come by; and as every one

believe, as, in fact, poor Chris himself have say, she have fling he

of malice prepense down on the line just as the train come up to they.



the workingman who give Bram most of these detail be the person who

disabuse Mr. Cornthwaite of his idea that the murderess be Claire. he

have give his information at the very time that Bram be on his way to

Hessel in the company of poor little Claire.



although Claire herself have not witness the catastrophe, she have

have the awful shock of come suddenly, a few minute later, upon the

mangle body of her die cousin. and Bram feel that he could not in

decency approach she with his own hope on his lip until she have in

some measure recover, not only from that shock, but from her father’s

death, and the loss of her beloved home.



the farm now look dreary in the extreme. April come, and it be still

unlet. the grass in the garden have grow high, the crocus be over,

and there be no one to tie up their long, thin, straggle leave. the

tulip be droop their petal, and the hyacinth be die. there

be nobody now to sow the seed for the summer.



Bram be on his way back home early one Saturday afternoon, when the

sun be shine brightly, show up the shabby condition of the house

and ground, the absence of paint on door and shutter, the weed

which be shoot up in the midst of the rubbish with which the

farmyard be block up.



as he lean over the garden gate and look ruefully in, with painful

thought about the little girl who be forget he in the society of

the curate, he fancy he hear a slight noise come from the house

itself.



he listen, he look. then he start erect. he grow red; his heart

begin to beat at express speed.



there be some one in the house, steal from room to room, not make

much noise. and from the glimpse he catch of a disappear figure in

its flight from one room to another Bram know that the intruder be

Claire.



he steal round to the back of the house with his heart on fire.



the door be lock; she have not get in that way. Bram have never give

up the workman’s habit of carry a few handy tool in a huge knife

in his pocket, and in a few second he have take one of the outside

kitchen shutter off its hinge, and shoot back the window-catch.



the next moment he be in the room.



but what a different room! the deal table where he have so often do

odd job of carpentering for Claire; the old sofa on which she have lie

on the night of Christian’s death while she utter those precious

word of love for himself, which he have treasure in his heart all

through the dark winter; the three-legged stool on which she use to

sit by the fire; the square, high one he use to occupy on the other

side--all these thing be go, and there be nothing in the bare and

dirty apartment but some odd and end of sack and a broken packing

case.



suddenly Bram conceive an idea. he drag the packing case over the

floor, take care not to make much noise, put it in the place of his

old stool, and sit down on it, bend over the dusty ashe which have

be leave in the fireplace just as he use to do over the fire on a

cold evening.



and presently the door open softly, and Claire come in.



he do not look round. he be satisfied to know that she be there,

there, almost within reach of his arm. and still he bend over the ashe.



a slight sob at last make he look up.



oh, what a sight for he! the little girl, look small than ever in

her black frock and bonnet, be stand in the full sunlight, smile

through her tear; smile with such unspeakable peace and happiness in

her eye, such a glint of joy illuminate her whole face, that as he

get up he stagger back, and cried--



“eh, Miss Claire, you’re more like a sunbeam than ever!”



she do not answer at first. she only clasp her small hand and

stare at he, with her lip part, and the tear spring to her

eye. but then she see something in his face which bring the blood to

her; and she turn quickly away, and pretend to find a difficulty

in make her way through the rubbish on the floor.



“Miss Claire!” say he. “oh, Miss Claire!”



that be the sum and substance of the eloquence he have be teach

himself; of the elaborate and carefully-choose word which he have so

often prepared to meet she with, word which should be respectful and

yet affectionate, sufficiently distant, yet not too cold. it have all

resolve itself into this hapless, helpless exclamation--



“Miss Claire! oh, Miss Claire!”



“I’m not surprised to find you here, Bram,” say she with a little

touch of grow reserve. “when I hear a noise in here I know I should

find you--just the same.”



there be a very short pause. then Bram say breathlessly--



“yes, Miss Claire, you’ll always find I just the same.”



the word, the tone, sum up all the kindness he have ever show she;

all the patient tenderness, the unspeakable, modest goodness she know

so well. Claire’s face quiver all over. then she burst into a torrent

of tear. Bram watch she for a minute in dead silence. then, not

dare so much as to come a step nearer, he whisper hoarsely--



“may I comfort you, Miss Claire, may I dare?”



“oh, Bram--dear Bram--if you don’t--I shall die!”



which, when you come to think of it, be a very pretty invitation.



and Bram accept it.



and they be marry, and they _be_ happy ever afterwards, though,

in these despondent day, it hardly do to say so.





the END.
